First Argument on Divisibility and Unity
First argument: if divisible not divided, unity would accept division; answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against the claim that a divisible body can remain one without actual division and the rebuttal that unity is a mental consideration, not a real accident.

Core Idea
Unity is a mental concept, not a real accident that divides.

This lesson examines a key debate in Islamic theology about the nature of unity and divisibility. The argument presented challenges the idea that a divisible thing can be one, and the answer clarifies that unity is not a physical attribute. We will follow the logical steps of the argument and its rebuttal as given by the author.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a premise, a necessary consequence, and a refutation. Focus on the logical flow.
The argument uses conditional reasoning: if A then B, but B is false, so A is false.
2
Understand the necessity
The argument claims that if a divisible thing is not actually divided, unity would be an accident in it. Since the substrate is divisible, the accident would have to divide as well. This would make unity accept division, which is contradictory.
The key point is that unity is defined as indivisibility, so accepting division contradicts its essence.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer provides two lines: first, unity is a mental consideration, not a real accident. Second, even if considered real, it is not a 'transitive accident' (عَرَض سارٍ) that divides with the substrate. Reflect on how this rebuts the argument.
This answer shows the importance of distinguishing between mental and real attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن القابل للانقسام لو لم يكن منقسماً بالفعل، بل واحداً في نفسه كما هو عند الحس لزم قبول الوحدة الانقسام، واللازم باطل إذ لا معنى لها سوى عدم الانقسام. وجه اللزوم أن الوحدة حينئذ تكون عارضة لذلك القابل حالة فيه، سواء جعلت لازمة له أو غير لازمة ضرورة أنها ليست نفسه، ولا جزءاً منه، وانقسام المحل يستلزم انقسام الحال ضرورة أن الحال في كل جزء غير الحال في الجزء الآخر. وأجيب بأن الوحدة من الاعتبارات العقلية، ولو سلّم فليست من الأعراض السارية التي تنقسم بانقسام المحل.
First: If that which is divisible is not actually divided but is one in itself as it is according to sense, then unity would necessarily accept division, and the consequent is false because unity has no meaning other than non-division. The necessity: then unity would be an accident in that substrate, whether considered necessary or not necessary, since it is neither its essence nor a part of it, and the division of the substrate necessitates the division of the accident because the accident in each part is different from the accident in the other part. And it was answered: unity is among mental considerations, and if granted, it is not among the transitive accidents that divide with the division of the substrate.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument against the claim that a divisible body can remain one without actual division. The argument assumes that if something is divisible but not actually divided, it is one. Then unity would have to accept division, which is impossible because unity means indivisibility. The necessity is explained: unity is an accident inherent in the substrate; dividing the substrate would require dividing the accident. The answer rebuts that unity is merely a mental consideration, not a real accident that spreads across parts. Even if considered real, it is not a transitive accident that divides with the substrate.

Key Terms
القابل للانقسام
الوحدة
الانقسام
عارضة
الأعراض السارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that unity is a real attribute that must divide when its substrate divides.
Correction: Unity is a mental consideration (i‘tibār ‘aqlī) and even if considered real, it is not a transitive accident that divides with the substrate.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given in this passage, why does the division of the substrate not necessarily entail the division of unity?
A) Because unity is a transitive accident.
B) Because unity is a mental consideration, not a real accident that divides.
C) Because unity is the essence of the substrate.
D) Because division does not affect accidents.

Key Takeaway
Unity is a mental consideration, not a real accident that divides with its substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you treat mental concepts as if they were physical realities, and what subtle confusion does that create in your understanding of God's attributes?
?
When you reflect on the unity of creation, do you ever mistakenly imagine that God's oneness is like a numerical unity that could be divided? How can you guard against that error?
?
The passage says unity is a mental consideration. In what areas of your faith might you be treating mental constructs as if they were tangible realities, and how can you realign your thinking with this insight?

The Sea and the Mosquito: Identity Through Change
Second argument: division annihilates unity; answer with sea example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the answer to the second argument defends the possibility of division without annihilation by distinguishing between identity as substance and identity as continuity.

Core Idea
Division need not annihilate identity if identity is in the substance, not the continuity.

The second argument against the unity of body claims that if a body is one, splitting it would destroy its identity and create two new ones. But we know a mosquito piercing the sea does not annihilate the sea. How is this possible? The following passage presents the argument, the objection, and a subtle answer that hinges on what we mean by 'the sea'.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a conditional claim, a justification, then a counterexample.
Pay attention to the terms 'هوية' (identity) and 'إعدام' (annihilation).
2
Identify the two answers
The answer gives two interpretations of 'sea': one with continuity, one without. Understand why each leads to a different conclusion.
The second interpretation aligns with the principle that the substrate remains.
3
Apply the principle of substrate
Reflect on the principle 'that which is receptive of a thing must remain with it'. How does this principle support the answer?
The water (substrate) remains even when continuity is broken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه لو كان واحداً لكان تقسيم الجسم وتفريق أجزائه إعداماً له، ضرورة أنه إزالة لهويته الواحدة، وإحداث لهويتين أخريين، واللازم باطل، للقطع بأن شق البعوض البحر بابرته ليس إعداماً له، وإحداثاً لبحرين أخريين. وأجيب بأنه إن أريد بالبحر ذلك الماء بما له من الاتصال، فلاخفاء في انعدامه عند عروض الانفصال، وإن أريد نفس ذلك الماء من غير اعتبار بالاتصال، فليس في الشق زوال بحر، ولا حدوث بحرين، وهذا أنسب بقواعدهم، حيث يقولون: إن القابل للشيء يجب أن يكون باقياً عنده، مجتمعاً معه.
Second: If it were one, then dividing the body and separating its parts would be its annihilation, because it is the removal of its single identity and the generation of two other identities, and the consequent is false, because it is certain that a mosquito piercing the sea with its proboscis is not its annihilation nor the generation of two other seas. And it was answered: If by 'sea' is meant that water with its connection, then there is no doubt about its annihilation upon the occurrence of separation; but if by 'sea' is meant that same water without consideration of connection, then in the piercing there is no removal of a sea nor occurrence of two seas, and this is more consistent with their principles, where they say: that which is receptive of a thing must remain with it and be united with it.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument: if a body is one, then splitting it annihilates its identity and creates two new identities, which is absurd because we know that splitting a sea (e.g., by a mosquito) does not annihilate the sea. The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'sea': if considered with its continuity, it is annihilated when separated; if considered as the water itself without continuity, it remains. This aligns with the principle that the substrate (قابل) must remain present with the form it receives.

Key Terms
تقسيم الجسم
هوية
إعدام
انفصال
قابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction made in the answer to the second argument regarding the sea example?
A) The sea is not actually divisible.
B) The sea's identity depends on its water, not its continuity.
C) The mosquito does not truly pierce the sea.
D) The sea's annihilation is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The second argument is answered by distinguishing between identity as continuity (which can be annihilated) and identity as substance (which remains).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you define your own identity by your continuous life story, or by your unchanging essence as a servant of Allah? What happens to your sense of self when your life is suddenly 'divided' by a trial or a sin? Does that change who you truly are before Allah?
?
The passage teaches that identity can survive division if it is in the substance, not the continuity. How does this apply to your relationship with Allah when you feel fragmented by distraction or disobedience? Will you cling to the continuity of perfect practice, or trust that your core of iman remains? What must you actively preserve?
?
If the same water remains after the mosquito pierces the sea, then your deeds—good and bad—remain with you even when life's circumstances separate you from your intentions. Are you prepared to face Allah with that unbroken record? What change in your daily habits does this truth demand?

The Third Argument: Actual Distinction of Parts
Third argument: if parts not actually distinct, their properties would not differ; answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for the actual distinction of parts and the response that properties are merely relational.

Core Idea
Properties like 'midpoint of half' are relational, not inherent – they arise only after mentally dividing.

This lesson continues the discussion on whether parts are actually or merely potentially distinct. Here the author presents an argument based on differing properties of assumed parts. If parts were not actually distinct, their properties would not differ. But we can see that the midpoint of a half is not the same as the midpoint of a third, suggesting actual distinction. The response challenges the causal link: these properties are relations that the mind imposes after assuming division.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the claim that if parts were not distinct, their properties would be identical, but they are not (e.g., midpoint of half vs. third).
The argument is labeled 'الرابع' but is actually the third in sequence.
2
Understand the response
Notice that the answer denies the necessary connection. It says the properties like half-ness are relations and considerations that the mind judges after assuming division. So the difference is not based on actual distinction.
The response also clarifies that the 'parts' are hypothetical (أجزاء فرضية) and can be described by real attributes like light and darkness.
3
Grasp the philosophical significance
This argument is a key point in the debate about parts and wholes in kalam. Understanding why differing properties do not prove actual distinction helps grasp the broader concept of potential versus actual existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: أن الأقسام لو لم تكن حاصلة بالفعل متميزة بعضها عن البعض لما اختلفت خواصها ضرورة، واللازم باطل، لأن مقطع النصف غير مقطع الثلث وكذا الربع والخمس وغيرهما، فيكون الجزء الذي هو مقطع النصف متميزاً عن الذي هو مقطع الربع وهكذا غيره. وأجيب بمنع الملازمة، فإن اختلاف الخواص إنما حصل بعد فرض الانقسام، وذلك أن النصفية، والثلثية، والربعية وغير ذلك إضافات واعتبارات يحكم بها العقل عند اعتبار الانقسام. وكذا مقاطعهما. فإن ادعي أن ما هو قابل لأن يكون مقطع النصف عند فرض الانقسام متميزاً بالفعل عما هو قابل لأن يكون مقطع الربع مثلاً، فهو نفس المتنازع، وحاصله أنه لا امتناع في اتصاف الأجزاء الفرضية بالصفات الحقيقية، كالضوء والظلام في القمر، فضلاً من الاعتبارية.
Fourth: If the parts were not actually present and distinct from each other, then necessarily their properties would not differ, and the consequent is false, because the midpoint of a half is not the midpoint of a third, likewise of a quarter, a fifth, etc. So the part that is the midpoint of half is distinct from the part that is the midpoint of a quarter, and so on. And it was answered by denying the necessary connection, for the difference of properties only occurs after assuming division, because half-ness, third-ness, quarter-ness, etc., are relations and considerations that the mind judges upon considering division. Likewise their midpoints. And if it is claimed that that which is capable of being the midpoint of a half upon assuming division is actually distinct from that which is capable of being the midpoint of a quarter, for example, then that is the very point of dispute. And its sum is that there is no impossibility in the imaginary parts being described by real attributes, like light and darkness in the moon, let alone by relational ones.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument: if parts are not actually distinct, they would not have different properties, but we know that the midpoint of a half differs from that of a quarter, implying actual distinction. The answer denies the necessary connection: properties like half-ness or quarter-ness are merely relations that the mind posits after assuming division. The properties are not inherent in the parts themselves but are relational. If the opponent insists that the 'capacities' for these midpoints are actually distinct, that is precisely what is being disputed. The answer concludes that it is possible for hypothetical parts to have real attributes (like light and dark areas on the moon), and even more so relational ones.

Key Terms
الأقسام
الخواص
مقطع
نصف
ثلث
إضافات
اعتبارات
أجزاء فرضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Different properties (like midpoint of half vs midpoint of third) prove that the parts are actually distinct before any mental division.
Correction: The properties are not inherent; they are relations (إضافات) that the mind imposes after assuming division. Thus, the difference does not imply actual distinction.

Checkpoint
How does the answer explain the difference between midpoints of different fractions (half, third, etc.)?
A) The parts are actually distinct.
B) The differences are real attributes of the parts.
C) The differences are relations that the mind considers after assuming division.
D) The differences are illusions.

Key Takeaway
The argument that differing properties prove actual distinction fails because those properties are merely relational, arising only after mental division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own character, do you treat your actions and moods as if they reveal permanent 'parts' of your soul (e.g., 'I am an angry person')? How does this lesson's insight that many attributes are merely relational and dependent on mental division challenge that assumption? What specific change in self-talk can you commit to today?
?
The argument in this passage shows that differences in properties do not necessarily indicate actual separate parts. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes believe that a single sin proves you are 'a sinner' by nature, rather than a believer who committed a sin? How can you apply the distinction between real and relational attributes to your view of your own spiritual state? Write down one step you will take to align your self-view with this lesson.

Objection on infinite divisibility and answer
Objection: infinite divisibility precludes fractions; answer distinguishing quantity from potential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why infinite divisibility does not preclude fractions in finite bodies, and how the distinction between potential and actual infinite parts resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Infinite divisibility is about potential, not actual infinite parts.

The objection claims that infinite divisions make fractions impossible. The answer reveals a crucial distinction: infinite divisibility in a finite body is a potential property, not an actual infinite quantity. This mirrors God's infinite power — it is about capacity, not actual infinite acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection states that if divisions are infinite, then a finite body cannot have a half, third, etc. Understand why this seems plausible.
Notice the logical flow: infinite divisibility → no definite fraction.
2
Grasp the answer's core move
The answer distinguishes between something infinite in quantity (continuous or discrete) and something finite in magnitude but receptive of infinite divisions.
The key is that the objector confuses actual infinity with potential infinity.
3
Distinguish potential from actual
Infinite divisibility does not mean that there are actually infinite parts; it means the body can be divided repeatedly without end, like God's power can create without end.
Potential infinite is about capacity, not realized infinity.
4
Apply the analogy
Consider the body's receptivity to division as analogous to God's ability to do more and more. Both are infinite in potential, not in actuality.
This analogy helps solidify the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال الانقسامات عندهم غير متناهية، وهو يستلزم لا تناهي الانقسام، وما لا نهاية له لا يتصور له نصف، أو ثلث، أو ربع، أو غيرها. لأنا نقول: إنما يمتنع ذلك فيما هو غير متناه بحسب كميته المتصلة، أو المنفصلة، وأما فيما متناهي المقدار لكنه قابل لانقسامات غير متناهية فلا، وإنما يمتنع لو كانت هناك أقسام بالفعل غير متناهية بالعدد، وليس كذلك، إذ ليس معنى قبول الجسم لانقسامات غير متناهية أنه يمكن خروجها من القوة إلى الفعل، بل إنه من شأنه وقوته أن ينقسم دائماً، ولا ينتهي دائماً، ولا ينتهي انقسامه إلى حد لا يمكن انقسامه، كما أن مقدورات الله تعالى غير متناهية، بمعنى أن قدرته لا تنتهي إلى حد لا يكون قادراً على أزيد منه، فليعتبر حال قابلية الجسم للانقسام إلى الأجزاء، بحال فاعلية
It should not be said: Divisions according to them are infinite, and this entails infinite divisibility, and that which has no end cannot be conceived to have a half, or a third, or a quarter, etc. For we say: That is only impossible in that which is infinite in terms of its continuous or discrete quantity. As for that which is finite in magnitude but receptive of infinite divisions, it is not [impossible]. It would be impossible only if there were actually infinite parts in number, but it is not so, for the meaning of the body's acceptance of infinite divisions is not that they can be brought from potentiality to actuality, but rather it is its property and power to be divided perpetually and never end, and its division does not end at a point where it cannot be divided, just as the things possible to God are infinite, meaning His power does not end at a limit where He is not able to do more. So consider the state of the body's receptivity for division into parts as the state of agency.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: if divisions are infinite, then a finite body cannot have specified fractions like half or third, because infinite divisions preclude definite proportions. The answer distinguishes between infinite in quantity (continuous or discrete), which would indeed prevent fractions, and finite magnitude with potential infinite divisibility. Infinite divisibility in a finite body means the process of division can never reach a final part; it is a property of potentiality, not actual infinite parts. The example of God's infinite power illustrates that infinite potential does not entail actual infinite parts. The body's receptivity to division is like that agency: endless in potential, not actual.

Key Terms
انقسامات غير متناهية
متناهي المقدار
قوة
فعل
مقدورات الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite divisibility means there are actually infinite parts inside a finite body.
Correction: Infinite divisibility means the process of division can continue indefinitely; there are not actually infinite parts, only the potential for division.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does infinite divisibility not prevent fractions in a finite body?
A) Because the body is actually infinite in size.
B) Because the divisions are only potential, not actual infinite parts.
C) Because fractions are defined arbitrarily.
D) Because the body has actual infinite parts but they are too small.

Key Takeaway
Infinite divisibility of a finite body does not entail actual infinite parts; it is a potential property, like God's infinite power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own potential—whether in skills, relationships, or spiritual growth—do you trust that God's power to bring it to actuality is unlimited, or do you unconsciously restrict Him by your own sense of finitude? What would it look like to live today as if God's power over your life is truly inexhaustible?
?
The passage likens infinite divisibility to God's power: He can always do more. Are there areas in your life where you have settled for a 'finite' view of God—believing He has reached His limit with you, or that He cannot change a certain situation? What step can you take right now to expand your expectation of His infinite capability?
?
If you truly believed that your failures and sins are not an 'actual infinite' block to God's mercy, but that His grace has an unlimited potential to cover them, how would your relationship with Him change today? Will you bring one specific area of shame before Him and let His power transform it from potential to reality?

The Motion Argument Against Indivisible Atoms
Taftazani elaborates the motion argument and its refinement against indivisible atoms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observation of different speeds in motion disproves the existence of indivisible atoms, and why the possibility of pauses does not rescue the atom.

Core Idea
Speed differences force space to be divisible.

Taftazani begins by asserting that certain absurdities are definitely impossible, such as the spontaneous disintegration of a solid body. He then presents the motion argument: if space were made of atoms, then when a fast mover covers one atom, a slower mover would either cover the same atom or more, leading to division of the atom. The only alternative is that the slower mover pauses between movements. However, even if pauses are allowed in theory, they lead to other impossibilities like the spontaneous disintegration of solid objects, which is known to be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read from 'الانتفاء قطعاً' to 'ثم التئامها' to understand the author's premise that certain things are impossible.
This sets the stage for the argument.
2
Understand the speed argument
Read the argument from 'بيان ذلك' to 'فينقسم الجزء' and note how the logic forces either equality or division of the atom.
This is the core of the motion argument.
3
Recognize the pause alternative
Read 'فلم يبق إلا أن يكون له في خلال حركاته سكنات' to see that the only way out is to posit pauses.
4
Explore why pauses are insufficient
Read from 'ولما كان هذا غير ممتنع' to 'كالفلك' to see that even with pauses, other absurdities arise, such as the disintegration of a stone or human.
These examples show the real force of the argument.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how these examples collectively disprove the existence of indivisible atoms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الانتفاء قطعاً، كتفكك أجزاء الجسم (۱) الذي في غاية الاستحكام لحظة فلحظة، ثم التئامها، وكتخيف المعلول عن العلة أو تحققه بدونها حيناً فحيناً، بيان ذلك أنا نجد المتوافقين في الأخذ والترك قد يتفاوتان في المسافة، فيحكم بأن الذي قطع مسافة أطول أسرع حركة ، والآخر أبطأ ، فلو كانت المسافة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، فعند قطع السريع جزءاً إما أن يقطع البطيء جزءاً فيه (۲) فيتساويان أو أكثر، فأبعد أو أقل، فينقسم الجزء ، فلم يبق إلا أن يكون له في خلال حركاته سكنات، ولما كان هذا غير ممتنع عند المتكلمين، بل مقرراً أعرضنا عنه إلى ما يكون تخلل (۳) السكنات فيه مستلزماً لما هو معلوم الانتفاء ، كتفكك أجزاء الجسم الذي هو مثل في الشدة والاستحكام كالحجر أو الذي لو تفككت أجزاؤه لتناثرت كالفرجار، أو كان له شعور بذلك، بل تبطل حبوته (٤) وحركته عند الأكثرين كالإنسان أو الذي ذهب جمع من العقلاء إلى امتناع تفككه كالفلك (٥) ، وكوجود العلة بدون المعلول في حركة الشمس مع سكون الظل، ووجود المعلول بدون علته في حركة الدلو إلى العلو، مع سكون حبل الكلاب، فيما إذا فرضنا بئراً عمقها مائة ذراع مثلاً، وفي منتصفها خشبة شد عليها طرف حبل طوله خمسون ذراعاً، وعلى طرفه الآخر دلو ثم شددنا كلاً بأعلى طرف حبل آخر طوله خمسون ذراعاً، وأرسلناه في البئر، بحيث وقع الكلاب في الحبل الأول على طرفه المشدود في الخشبة، ثم جررناه من البئر، فيكون ابتداء حركة الكلاب من الوسط والدلو من الأسفل معاً وكذا انتهاؤهما إلى رأس البئر وقد قطع الدلو مائة ذراع، والكلاب خمسين، مع أن حركة الكلاب من تمام علة حركة الدلو، فلو كان له سكنات في خلال حركته لزم وجود المعلول بدون علته التامة .
The impossibility is certain, like the disintegration of the parts of a body (1) that is in the utmost solidity moment by moment, then their recomposition, and like the removal of the effect from the cause or its existence without it from time to time. The explanation of that is that we find two [objects] agreeing in taking and leaving may differ in distance, so it is judged that the one who covered a longer distance is faster in motion, and the other slower. So if the distance were composed of indivisible parts, then when the fast one cuts a part, either the slow one cuts a part in it (2) so they are equal, or more, further or less, so the part divides. So nothing remains except that it has pauses (sakanāt) in the course of its motions. Since this is not impossible according to the mutakallimūn, but rather established, we turn aside from it to what has the interpenetration (takhallul) (3) of pauses entailing something known to be impossible, like the disintegration of the parts of a body that is an example of strength and solidity like a stone, or one whose parts if they disintegrated would scatter like a compass, or one that has sensation of that, rather its life (ḥubūtuhu) (4) and motion are invalidated according to most, like a human, or one about which a group of rational people have held the impossibility of its disintegration like the sphere (al-falak) (5). And like the existence of the cause without the effect in the motion of the sun with the stillness of the shadow, and the existence of the effect without its cause in the motion of the bucket upward with the stillness of the rope of the bucket, when we suppose a well whose depth is, for example, one hundred cubits, and in its middle a stick to which is tied the end of a rope fifty cubits long, and on its other end a bucket, then we tied each to the upper end of another rope fifty cubits long, and let it down into the well such that the bucket fell on the first rope at its end tied to the stick, then we pulled it from the well. So the beginning of the motion of the bucket from the middle and the bucket from the bottom is simultaneous, and likewise their arrival at the top of the well, while the bucket has covered one hundred cubits and the bucket [sic: the bucket's rope?] fifty, even though the motion of the bucket is part of the complete cause of the motion of the bucket [sic: the rope's motion is the cause of the bucket's motion]. So if it had pauses during its motion, the existence of the effect without its complete cause would follow.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction against the existence of indivisible atoms (al-juz' alladhī lā yatajazza'). The author begins by stating that the absurdity is definite, giving examples: the disintegration and recomposition of a solid body, and the separation of an effect from its cause. Then he explains the argument from motion: if space were composed of atoms, then when a fast mover covers one atom, a slower mover would either cover the same atom (equal) or more, leading to division of the atom—impossible. Therefore, the slower must have pauses (sakanāt) between its motions. But this is not impossible per se, so the author sets it aside and instead focuses on cases where such pauses entail obvious impossibilities. These include: the spontaneous disintegration of a solid body (like stone), the scattering of a compass, the loss of life and motion in a human, and the impossibility of the sphere disintegrating. Then he presents a causal argument: in the motion of the sun with the stillness of the shadow, we have the cause (sun's motion) without the effect (shadow's motion). More elaborate is the bucket-and-well example: a bucket is tied to a rope that passes over a stick in the middle of a well, and another rope is attached to the first rope's end. When pulled, the bucket (attached to the second rope) moves upward from the bottom while the first rope (the 'bucket' in the text) moves from the middle. They both start and end simultaneously, yet the bucket covers 100 cubits and the first rope only 50. Since the motion of the first rope is the complete cause of the bucket's motion, if the first rope had pauses, then for moments the bucket would be moving without its cause—an absurdity. Thus, atoms cannot exist.

Key Terms
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
السكنات
تفكك
الفلك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument relies on the impossibility of pauses, but the author explicitly states that pauses are not impossible for mutakallimūn, and instead uses the absurd consequences of pauses.
Correction: The argument's force is not in denying pauses but in showing that pauses lead to other absurdities that are definitely impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the motion argument, if a fast mover covers one atom, what must happen to a slower mover?
A) It must also cover the same atom, making them equal.
B) It must cover a different atom, dividing the space.
C) It must have pauses in its motion.
D) It must move faster to keep up.

Key Takeaway
The motion argument shows that the assumption of indivisible atoms leads to contradictions in observed motion and to absurd consequences like spontaneous disintegration of solid bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument forces you to follow a chain of reasoning to its logical end, even if it challenges your assumptions. Think of a deeply held belief about your own life or faith that you have never logically examined. What would it take for you to question it, as Taftazani does with atoms? Will you commit to examining one such belief this week?
?
Taftazani uses examples like a stone disintegrating to show that false premises lead to absurdities. Consider an area of your life where your actions contradict your stated beliefs—what 'disintegration' do you see? What step are you willing to take today to stop that contradiction, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
The argument requires you to trust that careful reasoning about observable phenomena can lead to certain knowledge about the unseen (the nature of space). How confident are you that your intellectual faculties can guide you to truth about matters like the existence of God or the afterlife? When was the last time you really relied on a reasoned argument rather than just feelings or tradition? Pause and commit to one specific area where you will apply rigorous reasoning this week.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
10 min
The Causal Argument Against Indivisible Atoms
Taftazani presents the causal argument against indivisible atoms using natural and constructed examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how examples of cause and effect, such as the motion of the sun and the bucket-and-well example, disprove the existence of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
Causality requires continuous connection, disallowing pauses.

Taftazani presents two causal challenges: first, the motion of the sun while the shadow remains still shows a cause without its effect. More elaborate is the bucket-and-well example, where the motion of a rope (the cause) and a bucket (the effect) are compared. If the rope had pauses, the bucket would move without its cause, which is impossible. Thus, indivisible atoms cannot exist because they would imply such pauses.

Guided Path
1
Read the causal examples
Read the two examples: the sun-shadow and the bucket-and-well. Notice how each illustrates a break in causation.
The first is simple; the second is detailed.
2
Understand the bucket example
Focus on the detailed description: a rope tied to a stick, a bucket, and the distances covered. See how the rope and bucket start and end together but cover different distances.
The rope is the cause, the bucket the effect.
3
Analyze the implication
The rope's motion is the complete cause of the bucket's motion. If the rope had pauses, the bucket would be moving without its cause during those pauses, which is impossible.
4
Conclude against atoms
Since the existence of atoms would require such pauses (as shown in the motion argument), and those pauses lead to impossible causal gaps, atoms cannot exist.
This complements the motion argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الانتفاء قطعاً، كتفكك أجزاء الجسم (۱) الذي في غاية الاستحكام لحظة فلحظة، ثم التئامها، وكتخيف المعلول عن العلة أو تحققه بدونها حيناً فحيناً، بيان ذلك أنا نجد المتوافقين في الأخذ والترك قد يتفاوتان في المسافة، فيحكم بأن الذي قطع مسافة أطول أسرع حركة ، والآخر أبطأ ، فلو كانت المسافة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، فعند قطع السريع جزءاً إما أن يقطع البطيء جزءاً فيه (۲) فيتساويان أو أكثر، فأبعد أو أقل، فينقسم الجزء ، فلم يبق إلا أن يكون له في خلال حركاته سكنات، ولما كان هذا غير ممتنع عند المتكلمين، بل مقرراً أعرضنا عنه إلى ما يكون تخلل (۳) السكنات فيه مستلزماً لما هو معلوم الانتفاء ، كتفكك أجزاء الجسم الذي هو مثل في الشدة والاستحكام كالحجر أو الذي لو تفككت أجزاؤه لتناثرت كالفرجار، أو كان له شعور بذلك، بل تبطل حبوته (٤) وحركته عند الأكثرين كالإنسان أو الذي ذهب جمع من العقلاء إلى امتناع تفككه كالفلك (٥) ، وكوجود العلة بدون المعلول في حركة الشمس مع سكون الظل، ووجود المعلول بدون علته في حركة الدلو إلى العلو، مع سكون حبل الكلاب، فيما إذا فرضنا بئراً عمقها مائة ذراع مثلاً، وفي منتصفها خشبة شد عليها طرف حبل طوله خمسون ذراعاً، وعلى طرفه الآخر دلو ثم شددنا كلاً بأعلى طرف حبل آخر طوله خمسون ذراعاً، وأرسلناه في البئر، بحيث وقع الكلاب في الحبل الأول على طرفه المشدود في الخشبة، ثم جررناه من البئر، فيكون ابتداء حركة الكلاب من الوسط والدلو من الأسفل معاً وكذا انتهاؤهما إلى رأس البئر وقد قطع الدلو مائة ذراع، والكلاب خمسين، مع أن حركة الكلاب من تمام علة حركة الدلو، فلو كان له سكنات في خلال حركته لزم وجود المعلول بدون علته التامة .

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the bucket example is too contrived to be a valid argument, but it illustrates a logical consequence of atomism.

Checkpoint
In the bucket-and-well example, what problem does the author identify if the first rope (cause) had pauses during its motion?
A) The bucket would move faster than the rope.
B) The bucket would move without its complete cause existing at that moment.
C) The rope would break.
D) The bucket would stop moving.

Key Takeaway
The causal argument demonstrates that indivisible atoms would require pauses in causation, which contradict observed cause-effect relationships.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever expect results (effects) without putting in the continuous effort (cause) – whether in studies, relationships, or spirituality? How does Taftazani's well example expose the logical flaw in that expectation?
?
If you believe that Allah's creation operates on continuous causes without gaps, how should that shape your patience when seeking outcomes? Are you tempted to demand immediate effects without trusting the causal process He has set?
?
Consider the bucket and the rope: the slower rope still moves continuously to cause the bucket's faster motion. In what areas of your faith or practice are you like the rope – moving slowly but steadily – and how does that encourage you to persist, knowing that your continuous effort is part of Allah's causal order?

Refuting Atomism: Conceptual vs Actual Indivisibility
Sanusi refutes the atomist claim that parts are indivisible only conceptually.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the atomist claim that parts are indivisible only conceptually, using the principle that what applies to the whole must apply to each part by essence.

Core Idea
Conceptual indivisibility does not prove actual indivisibility — accidental barriers are not essential.

This lesson covers Sanusi's refutation of the ancient atomist doctrine that bodies consist of tiny, hard particles that cannot actually separate but are only conceptually divisible. Sanusi exposes the logical flaw: if the parts share the same nature, the impossibility of actual division must be due to accidental causes, not essential indivisibility. The argument is a classic example of kalam reasoning about the nature of bodies and parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Note the structure: refutation statement, description of atomism, logical exposition, and answer to objection.
The passage is dense; reading slowly helps.
2
Identify the atomist claim
The atomists claim bodies consist of small, hard, similar particles that are indivisible in actual separation (انفكاك) but divisible conceptually (وهماً). Understand this distinction.
This is the view of Democritus.
3
Understand Sanusi's refutation principle
Sanusi argues that since the parts are equal in nature (متشابهة الطبع), whatever is possible for the aggregate must be possible for each part by essence. Therefore, if the aggregate cannot be split actually, the parts cannot be essentially indivisible.
The impossibility of actual division for the whole would then apply to each part.
4
See how accidental barriers are addressed
Sanusi admits that actual division may be prevented by accidents like smallness, hardness, or lack of tools, but this does not make the part essentially indivisible. It only prevents actual separation.
This answers the objection that parts seem indivisible.
5
Review the dismissal of al-Razi's objection
The passage ends by rejecting an objection that bodily extensions change upon separation. Sanusi says such individuated forms may prevent the common essence from acting, not that division is impossible.
This reinforces the accidental nature of indivisibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ثم إنهم أبطلوا) كون الجسم من أجزاء لا تتجزأ وهماً لا فعلاً بأنها لما كانت متساوية في الطبع بزعمهم جاز على كل ما جاز على الكل بحسب الذات، وإن امتنع بعارض تشخص أو غيره. يشير إلى إبطال ما ذهب إليه ديمقراطيس وجمع من القدماء، من أن ما يشاهد من الأجسام المفردة كالماء مثلاً ليست ببسائط على الإطلاق، بل إنما هي حاصلة من تماس بسائط صغار متشابهة الطبع، في غاية الصلابة، غير قابلة للقسمة الانفكاكية، بل الوهمية فقط وبهذا وبتسميتها أجساماً يمتاز هذا المذهب عن مذهب القائلين بالجزء ، وتقريره أن تلك الأجزاء لما كانت متشابهة الطبع باعترافهم جاز على كل منها ما جاز على الآخرين، وعلى المجموع الحاصل من اجتماعها، والقسمة الانفكاكية مما لا يجوز على المجموع ، فيجوز على كل جزء، إذ لو امتنعت على الجزء نظراً إلى ذاته لامتنعت على المجموع ، ثم إمكان الانفكاك نظراً إلى الذات لا ينافي امتناعه لعارض تشخص أو غيره، من صور نوعية أو غاية صغر أو صلابة ، أو عدم آلة قطاعة ، أو نحو ذلك فلا يرد اعتراض الإمام بأن الامتدادات الجسمية غير باقية عند الانفصال، ومتجردة عند الاتصال، فهي أمور متشخصة، ولعلها تمنع الماهية المشتركة عن فعلها.
He said: (Then they refuted) that the body is composed of parts that are indivisible conceptually but not actually, because when they are equal in nature according to their claim, whatever is allowed for the whole is allowed for each by essence, even if it is prevented by an accident of individuation or something else. This refers to refuting what Democritus and a group of the ancients went to, that what is observed of individual bodies like water are not absolutely simple, but rather they result from contact of small simples similar in nature, extremely hard, not susceptible to separation-division, but only conceptual division. By this and by calling them bodies, this doctrine is distinguished from the doctrine of those who speak of the part. Its exposition: since those parts are similar in nature by their admission, whatever is allowed for each is allowed for the others, and for the aggregate resulting from their union; separation-division is not allowed for the aggregate, so it is allowed for each part. For if it were impossible for the part by its essence, it would be impossible for the aggregate. Then the possibility of separation by essence does not contradict its impossibility due to an accident of individuation or something else, such as specific forms, extreme smallness, hardness, lack of cutting instrument, or the like. So the objection of the Imam that bodily extensions are not permanent upon separation and are abstract upon connection does not apply, for they are individuated things, and perhaps they prevent the common essence from its act.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the atomist doctrine as held by Democritus and other ancients. The atomists claim that observed bodies like water are not truly simple but consist of small, hard, similar particles that are indivisible only in actual separation (انفكاك) but are conceptually divisible. Sanusi refutes this by analyzing the principle: if all parts share the same nature, then what is possible for the whole (the aggregate) must also be possible for each part by essence. Since the aggregate cannot undergo separation-division (because it is a continuous body), the parts likewise should not be able to separate. However, the atomists admit that the parts are actually not separate (only conceptually divisible). The refutation shows that the impossibility of separation for the parts is not due to their essence but due to accidental causes like extreme smallness, hardness, or lack of a cutting tool. This does not contradict their essential divisibility. The passage also dismisses an objection from 'the Imam' (likely al-Razi) about bodily extensions not persisting during separation, arguing that individuation may prevent the common essence from manifesting division. The logical structure: 1) State the atomist position. 2) Expose its inconsistency: if parts are equal in nature, they must share the same modal properties. 3) Show that the lack of actual division is due to accidental barriers, not essential indivisibility. 4) Conclude that the atomist position is thereby refuted.

Key Terms
وهم
فعل
الانفكاك
القسمة الانفكاكية
تماس
بسائط
متشابهة الطبع
عارض تشخص
الامتدادات الجسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that conceptual indivisibility (وهماً) is the same as essential indivisibility.
Correction: The refutation shows that conceptual indivisibility is merely accidental; the parts are essentially divisible, but external barriers prevent actual separation.

Checkpoint
What principle does Sanusi use to refute the atomist claim that parts are indivisible only conceptually?
A) Parts are not similar in nature.
B) What is possible for the aggregate is possible for each part by essence.
C) Actual division is impossible for all bodies.
D) Conceptual division implies essential division.

Key Takeaway
The atomist claim of conceptual indivisibility is refuted by the principle that essential properties of the whole must apply to each part, so actual indivisibility cannot be essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that what seems like an essential limitation may actually be an accidental barrier. Think of an area in your life—your prayer, your patience, your knowledge—where you have told yourself, 'I just can't do this,' believing it is part of your nature. Is it truly essential, or have you mistaken an accidental obstacle for an essential lack? What would you need to remove that barrier, and will you commit to removing it starting today?
?
The atomists clung to a false theory because they stopped at how things appeared—conceptually indivisible—rather than probing deeper to find the accidental cause. When have you settled for a shallow understanding of a religious matter, content with a surface-level explanation that felt 'obvious'? Are you willing today to go deeper, even if it means unsettling comfortable assumptions?
?
Sanusi shows that the whole is not actually divided, so each part must be divisible by essence—but the part appears indivisible due to accidents. In your own spiritual life, the 'whole' of your community or ummah may appear disunited, but you might blame individuals as 'indivisible' in their faults. Could your judgment be missing the accidental circumstances that shape them? Will you choose to see beyond the accidents and treat each person as essentially capable of change, just as you hope others see you?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Necessity of a Receptive Substrate for Change in Contiguity
Answer explaining the necessity of a persisting substrate for changes in contiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the change from one state of contiguity to another requires a persisting substrate that remains receptive to both states.

Core Idea
Change in contiguity requires a permanent substrate that endures through the change.

This passage presents an answer to an objection regarding the nature of non-contiguity. It clarifies that the absence of contiguity is not absolute nothingness but a transition from one mode of contiguity to two. For such a transition to be intelligible, there must be a substrate that persists and is capable of receiving both states. This substrate is matter (hayula). The answer underscores a key principle in Islamic metaphysics: all accidental changes require an underlying substance that remains identical through the change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Observe how the answer begins by negating an absolute notion of non-contiguity.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice that the answer states that non-contiguity is not absolute but rather of something capable of contiguity. This 'something' is the receptive substrate.
3
Understand the logical structure
The answer explains the change as the removal of one contiguity-identity and the occurrence of two contiguity-identities. This requires a persisting subject.
4
Connect to broader doctrine
Why does this matter? It establishes that material substances are necessary for accidental changes, a foundational principle in kalam and falsafa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أنه ليس عدم الاتصال مطلقاً، بل عما من شأنه الاتصال وهو المعنى بالقابل الباقي بل هو عدم اتصال إلى اتصالين أي زوال هوية اتصالية، وحدوث هويتين اتصاليتين، فلا بد من أمر قابل للاتصال تارة، وللاتصالين أخرى.
And the answer: that it is not absolute non-contiguity, but rather [non-contiguity] of that which is capable of contiguity, and that is the meaning of 'the remaining receptive substrate'; rather, it is the loss of one contiguity [and arrival] at two contiguities, i.e., the removal of one contiguity-identity and the occurrence of two contiguity-identities. So there must be something receptive of contiguity at one time, and of two contiguities at another.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection about the nature of non-contiguity. It clarifies that the absence of contiguity referred to is not absolute non-existence but the change from a state of one contiguity to a state of two contiguities. For such a change to occur, there must be a persisting substrate (the 'remaining receptive substrate') that remains the same through the change and is capable of receiving both states. This substrate is matter (hayula), which is neither essentially contiguous nor separate but receives these determinations. The argument establishes the need for a substrate that endures while accidental properties (contiguity and separation) change.

Key Terms
اتصال
انفصال
قابل
هوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what does the change from one contiguity to two contiguities require?
A) Absolute non-existence
B) A remaining receptive substrate
C) Creation ex nihilo
D) The destruction of the body

Key Takeaway
For a change in contiguity to be intelligible, a persisting substrate must remain to receive the new state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something in your life you thought was lost forever—a relationship, a skill, a sense of self. How does knowing that change requires a persisting substrate challenge you to look for what remains, even when everything seems to have fallen apart? Will you search for the constant that Allah has placed in your heart, or will you let the change define you as lost?
?
Are you aware of your own tendency to treat sudden changes in your life as absolute destruction? This lesson teaches that change is a transformation of accidents, not annihilation of the subject. How does this reshape your fear of death? When you contemplate your own mortality, can you trust that your core self, your soul, persists under Allah's care, even when the contiguity of your body changes? What are you holding onto that you should release?
?
The receptive substrate here is matter, but for you, your soul is the substrate that receives Allah's commands and trials. Are you conscious of your soul as the constant that must remain steadfast through every change in your circumstances? What one change in your character do you need to undergo, knowing that your soul remains to receive a new state? Will you commit today to seeking that change?

Third Objection and Answer on the Infinite Regress of Matter
Third objection and its answer concerning the infinite regress of matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third objection against prime matter (hayula) is resolved by distinguishing between essentially contiguous subjects and neutral substrates.

Core Idea
Matter does not need another matter because it is not essentially contiguous.

This lesson examines the third objection that threatens an infinite regress of matter. The objection claims that if the capacity to receive separation requires a material substrate, then matter itself would require another matter. The answer resolves this by showing that the proof applies only to essentially contiguous things, not to the neutral substrate that is prime matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage which states the objection: if reception of separation necessitates matter, then matter itself would need another matter ad infinitum because it also receives separation.
Notice the logical structure: if X then Y, leading to infinite regress.
2
Identify the answer's key distinction
The answer distinguishes between things that are essentially contiguous (like form and body) and matter, which is not essentially contiguous. This distinction stops the regress.
The key term 'hayula' refers to prime matter, the ultimate substrate.
3
Understand the verification
Read the verification section explaining that what is essentially contiguous requires a subject that is neither essentially contiguous nor separate, but can receive both properties while remaining the same.
This confirms that matter is the needed neutral substrate.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Ask yourself why this distinction prevents infinite regress. Because matter, as the neutral receiver, does not itself need another matter — it is the endpoint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان قبول الانفصال محوجاً إلى المادة لاحتاجت المادة إلى مادة أخرى لا إلى نهاية ضرورة قبولها الانفصال. وجوابه : إن المحوج هو قبول الانفصال فيما يكون متصلاً بذاته، كالصورة والجسم وليست الهيولي كذلك، وتحقيقه أن ما يكون متصلاً في ذاته الانفصال، فيفتقر إلى أمر لا يكون متصلاً في ذاته ولا منفصلاً، بل يتوارد عليه الاتصال والانفصال وهو هو بعينه في الحالين يصير واحداً متصلاً بعروض الوحدة والاتصال ومتعدداً منفصلاً بعروض الكثرة والانفصال من غير افتقار إلى ورود أمر آخر.
Third: If the reception of separation necessitates matter, then matter would need other matter ad infinitum because it necessarily receives separation. And its answer: That which necessitates is the reception of separation in what is essentially contiguous, like form and body, and matter is not like that. Its verification: that which is essentially contiguous [receives] separation; then it needs something that is not essentially contiguous nor separate, but rather contiguity and separation occur upon it, and it is exactly the same in both states, becoming one and contiguous by the occurrence of unity and contiguity, and multiple and separate by the occurrence of multiplicity and separation, without needing the advent of another thing.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its answer. The objection argues that if the capacity to receive separation (a property of bodies) requires a material substrate, then matter itself, since it also receives separation (as it takes on different forms), would need another matter, leading to an infinite regress. The answer distinguishes between two types of receiving subjects: those that are essentially contiguous (like form and body) and those that are not. Matter (hayula) is not essentially contiguous; it is a neutral substrate that can receive both contiguity and separation without being essentially either. Therefore, it does not require another matter to ground its receptivity. The verification explains that what is essentially contiguous needs a subject that is not essentially contiguous or separate, but rather one that can take on both properties while remaining the same subject. This preserves matter as the ultimate substrate.

Key Terms
مادة
هيولي
صورة
جسم
اتصال
انفصال
قبول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if matter receives separation, it would need another matter because the same rule applies to everything.
Correction: The answer clarifies that the proof applies only to things essentially contiguous; matter is not essentially contiguous, so it does not require a further substrate.

Checkpoint
Why does the third objection about infinite regress not apply to prime matter?
A) Because matter does not receive separation.
B) Because matter is essentially contiguous.
C) Because the proof applies only to things essentially contiguous, and matter is not.
D) Because infinite regress is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Prime matter is the ultimate substrate because it is not essentially contiguous, thus it does not need another matter to ground its receptivity of forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that prime matter is neutral, able to receive both good and evil forms. In your own life, what forms have you allowed to define you? Are you taking on the form of submission to Allah, or have you let the forms of this world shape you? Commit to one concrete change to let the form of faith dominate.
?
You claim to believe that Allah is the only necessary being. But do you sometimes behave as if material things are independent causes? How does the lesson’s grounding of matter in a neutral substrate (which itself points to a Creator) reframe your reliance on material means? Write one practical step to trust Allah more than physical causes.
?
The objection in the passage could have led to an infinite regress, but the answer stops it. What assumptions or habits in your life lead to mental regress? Where do you need to stop and accept a foundational truth (like tawheed)? Identify one endless worry and replace it with trust in Allah’s sole causality.

Fourth Objection: Body's Receptivity and the Two Kinds of Contiguity
Fourth objection and initial answer distinguishing two types of contiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against contiguity being a part of the body and the author's distinction between essential and accidental contiguity that resolves the dilemma.

Core Idea
Contiguity is twofold: essential (part of body) and accidental (separable).

The fourth objection argues that if contiguity is a part of the body, the body cannot be receptive to separation, and vice versa. The author answers by distinguishing two kinds of contiguity: essential extension that is permanently part of the body, and accidental extension that is a separable quantity. This distinction preserves the body's ability to change while remaining a single substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and dilemma
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is presented. Note how it sets up a contradiction: if contiguity is part of the body, separation would annihilate the body; if the body is receptive to separation, contiguity cannot be a part.
The objection uses the logical structure of conditional statements.
2
Identify the answer's distinction
The answer introduces a key distinction between essential contiguity (essential extension) and accidental contiguity (accidental quantity). Understand each type and its relation to the body.
Essential contiguity is inseparable; accidental contiguity can change.
3
Follow the rebuttal of the dilemma
The author shows which contiguity actually vanishes when separation occurs. If it is essential, the body would be destroyed; but it is accidental, so the body remains and is receptive.
This disproves the claim that body cannot be receptive.
4
Reflect on the candle example
Consider the example of a candle: its shape changes while the corporeality (body) remains. This illustrates how accidental extension changes over a persisting substance.
The candle's changing extents correspond to accidental contiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن كون الاتصال جزءاً من الجسم ينافي كون الجسم قابلاً للاتصال والانفصال، لأن الأول يستلزم انعدام الجسم عند زوال الاتصال، والثاني يستلزم بقاء عنده ضرورة اجتماع القابل مع المقبول، فحينئذ يتوجه أن يقال لو كان الاتصال جزءاً لم يكن الجسم قابلاً للانفصال، وقد قلتم ببطلان اللازم، أو يقال لو كان الجسم قابلاً له لم يكن الاتصال جزءاً، وقد قلتم بحقية الملزوم، وهكذا في الجانب الآخر لا يقال الاتصال يطلق بالإشراك أو المجاز، على امتداد جوهري، هو الجزء وليس بزايل عن الجسم، بل ينعدم الجسم بانعدامه وعلى عرضي هو الزائل عن الجسم، وليس بجزء له، بل كمية عارضة. لأنا نقول : الاتصال الذي يزول بطريان الانفصال إن كان هو الأول لم يكن القابل للانفصال هو الجسم لانعدامه حينئذ، فيبطل قولكم في إثبات الهيولي: أن الجسم قابل للاتصال والانفصال، وإن كان هو الثاني لم ينعدم الجسم بانعدامه، فلم يمتنع كونه قابلاً للانفصال بذاته من غير افتقار إلى الهيولي، لا يقال الامتداد العرضي من لوازم الجسمية، فزواله بزوالها، لأنا نقول : الزائل امتداد مخصوص وليس بلازم واللازم امتداد ما وليس بزائل، كما يرى في الشمعة تبدل المقادير مع بقاء الجسمية بعينها، لا يقال فكذلك الامتداد الجوهري لأنا نقول هذا لا يضر بالمقصود، بل يفيده لأن ما يزول عنه خصوص امتداد ويطرأ آخران هو المعنى بالهيولي ولذا قالوا : كما تتبدل المقادير على جوهر باق هو الصورة تتبدل الصورة على جوهر باق هو المادة، بل الجواب : أن ليس معنى قبول الجسم للانفصال أنه بقائه بجميع أجزائه يقبله، بل إن فيه جزءاً باقياً بعينه هو القابل بالحقيقة له وللاتصال الذي يقابله أما عدم الزوال بالكلية فلضرورة التفرقة بين انعدام ماء الجرة بالمرة وبين انفصاله إلى مياه جمة، وأما عدم البقاء بتمام الماهية واقتصار الزوال على الهوية فلانعدام الجزء الذي هو الاتصال هذا. والإنصاف أن انفصال الماء إلى المياه ليس بانعدام جوهر وحدوث آخر، وأن الباقي في الحالين هو الماء بحقيقته، وإن تبدل في هويته لا جزء منها هو الهيولي.
Fourth: That contiguity being a part of the body is incompatible with the body being receptive to contiguity and separation, because the first entails the annihilation of the body when contiguity ceases, and the second entails its subsistence necessarily requiring the conjunction of the receptive with the received. So then it may be said: if contiguity were a part, the body would not be receptive to separation, and you have said the consequent is false; or it may be said: if the body were receptive to it, then contiguity would not be a part, and you have said the antecedent is true. And likewise on the other side, it is said that contiguity is applied by common usage or metaphor to essential extension, which is the part and does not separate from the body, rather the body is annihilated by its annihilation; and to accidental extension, which is that which separates from the body, and is not a part of it, but rather an accidental quantity. So we say: the contiguity that is removed by the occurrence of separation, if it is the first, then the receptive of separation would not be the body because of its annihilation at that time, thus your statement in proving matter—that the body is receptive to contiguity and separation—is invalid; and if it is the second, the body is not annihilated by its annihilation, so there is no obstacle to it being receptive to separation by itself without needing matter. It cannot be said that accidental extension is a necessary concomitant of corporeality, and so its removal would be by the removal of corporeality, because we say: what is removed is a particular extension, not a necessary concomitant, and the necessary concomitant is extension as such and is not removed, as is seen in a candle where extents change while corporeality remains the same. It cannot be said: likewise for essential extension, because we say this does not harm the intended point, rather it benefits it, because that from which a particular extension is removed and two others occur is the meaning of matter. Thus they said: just as extents change over a remaining substance which is form, so do forms change over a remaining substance which is matter. Rather, the answer: that the meaning of the body's reception of separation is not that it receives it while remaining with all its parts, but rather that in it there is a part remaining itself which is the true receptive of it and of the contiguity that is opposite to it. As for the absence of complete removal, it is due to the necessity of distinguishing between the annihilation of the water of a jar entirely and its separation into many waters. As for the absence of remaining with the complete essence and the restriction of removal to identity, it is due to the annihilation of the part which is this contiguity. The fair truth is that the separation of water into many waters is not an annihilation of a substance and the occurrence of another, and that what remains in both states is water in its reality, even if it changes in its identity—there is no part of it that is matter.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and its lengthy answer. The objection argues that if contiguity is a part of the body, then the body cannot be receptive to both contiguity and separation: if contiguity is a part, its removal would destroy the body, so the body cannot receive separation; conversely, if the body is receptive to separation, then contiguity cannot be a part. The answer begins by distinguishing two senses of contiguity: essential contiguity (the substantial extension that is a part of the body and inseparable) and accidental contiguity (a separable accidental quantity). The contiguity that vanishes upon separation is accidental, not essential. Thus, when a body divides, the accidental extension changes while the essential substrate (the body as a substance) remains. The answer then addresses potential objections using examples: a candle's size changes while its corporeality remains; similarly, the persisting substrate of change is matter (hayula). Finally, the author refines the notion: the body's reception of separation does not mean all parts remain; rather, a part (the substrate) persists while the contiguity-part perishes. Using the water-in-a-jar example, separation is not annihilation of substance but a change in identity, and the underlying reality (water) remains. The conclusion affirms that matter is not a part of the body's essence but the substratum of change.

Key Terms
اتصال
انفصال
جسم
امتداد جوهري
امتداد عرضي
هيولي
صورة
مادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If any kind of contiguity is part of the body, then the body cannot undergo separation.
Correction: Only essential contiguity is part; accidental contiguity is separable, so the body can change its form.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what kind of contiguity is considered a part of the body?
A) Essential extension (al-imtidad al-jawhari)
B) Accidental extension (al-imtidad al-'aradi)
C) Both A and B
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The body remains one substance despite losing accidental contiguity; what persists is a substrate (form/matter).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'accidental extensions' in your life—your status, wealth, relationships—do you treat as essential to your identity? If they were removed, would you still know who you are? Take a moment to name one, and ask yourself: can I let that go without losing myself?
?
The candle changes size but remains the same body. What parts of you have changed over the years—your opinions, habits, even your faith journey—yet you are still you? Do you anchor your worth in what changes or in what abides?
?
If your body's persistence through change mirrors matter's persistence through forms, what does that say about your own mortality? Does the thought that you are a substrate of change give you peace or unease? Will you today act as if your true self is what remains, not what passes away?

Refining Receptivity: Substratum and the Water Example
Refined meaning of body's receptivity to separation and the water example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refined meaning of the body's reception of separation and the role of matter as a persisting substratum, illustrated by the water example.

Core Idea
Separation is change in identity, not substance; matter is the substratum of change.

Having established the distinction between essential and accidental contiguity, the author further refines what it means for the body to be receptive to separation. He clarifies that the body does not remain with all its parts; rather, a persisting part (the substratum) remains while the contiguity-part perishes. The example of water separating into many waters illustrates that the underlying reality (water) remains, even though the identity changes. This leads to the conclusion that matter is not a part of the body's essence but the substratum of substantial change.

Guided Path
1
Read the refined answer
Read the passage beginning with 'بل الجواب' which gives the final answer. Notice that the author redefines what 'reception of separation' means.
He distinguishes between complete removal and change in identity.
2
Understand the water analogy
Compare the annihilation of water in a jar (complete destruction) with its separation into many waters (change in identity). The water persists in reality but its identity changes.
This shows that separation does not annihilate the substance.
3
Identify the role of matter
The passage concludes that what remains through change is the 'reality' of the substance, not its identity, and there is no part of it that is matter. Matter is the substratum of essential change, not a component.
This connects back to the hayula debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن كون الاتصال جزءاً من الجسم ينافي كون الجسم قابلاً للاتصال والانفصال، لأن الأول يستلزم انعدام الجسم عند زوال الاتصال، والثاني يستلزم بقاء عنده ضرورة اجتماع القابل مع المقبول، فحينئذ يتوجه أن يقال لو كان الاتصال جزءاً لم يكن الجسم قابلاً للانفصال، وقد قلتم ببطلان اللازم، أو يقال لو كان الجسم قابلاً له لم يكن الاتصال جزءاً، وقد قلتم بحقية الملزوم، وهكذا في الجانب الآخر لا يقال الاتصال يطلق بالإشراك أو المجاز، على امتداد جوهري، هو الجزء وليس بزايل عن الجسم، بل ينعدم الجسم بانعدامه وعلى عرضي هو الزائل عن الجسم، وليس بجزء له، بل كمية عارضة. لأنا نقول : الاتصال الذي يزول بطريان الانفصال إن كان هو الأول لم يكن القابل للانفصال هو الجسم لانعدامه حينئذ، فيبطل قولكم في إثبات الهيولي: أن الجسم قابل للاتصال والانفصال، وإن كان هو الثاني لم ينعدم الجسم بانعدامه، فلم يمتنع كونه قابلاً للانفصال بذاته من غير افتقار إلى الهيولي، لا يقال الامتداد العرضي من لوازم الجسمية، فزواله بزوالها، لأنا نقول : الزائل امتداد مخصوص وليس بلازم واللازم امتداد ما وليس بزائل، كما يرى في الشمعة تبدل المقادير مع بقاء الجسمية بعينها، لا يقال فكذلك الامتداد الجوهري لأنا نقول هذا لا يضر بالمقصود، بل يفيده لأن ما يزول عنه خصوص امتداد ويطرأ آخران هو المعنى بالهيولي ولذا قالوا : كما تتبدل المقادير على جوهر باق هو الصورة تتبدل الصورة على جوهر باق هو المادة، بل الجواب : أن ليس معنى قبول الجسم للانفصال أنه بقائه بجميع أجزائه يقبله، بل إن فيه جزءاً باقياً بعينه هو القابل بالحقيقة له وللاتصال الذي يقابله أما عدم الزوال بالكلية فلضرورة التفرقة بين انعدام ماء الجرة بالمرة وبين انفصاله إلى مياه جمة، وأما عدم البقاء بتمام الماهية واقتصار الزوال على الهوية فلانعدام الجزء الذي هو الاتصال هذا. والإنصاف أن انفصال الماء إلى المياه ليس بانعدام جوهر وحدوث آخر، وأن الباقي في الحالين هو الماء بحقيقته، وإن تبدل في هويته لا جزء منها هو الهيولي.
Fourth: That contiguity being a part of the body is incompatible with the body being receptive to contiguity and separation, because the first entails the annihilation of the body when contiguity ceases, and the second entails its subsistence necessarily requiring the conjunction of the receptive with the received. So then it may be said: if contiguity were a part, the body would not be receptive to separation, and you have said the consequent is false; or it may be said: if the body were receptive to it, then contiguity would not be a part, and you have said the antecedent is true. And likewise on the other side, it is said that contiguity is applied by common usage or metaphor to essential extension, which is the part and does not separate from the body, rather the body is annihilated by its annihilation; and to accidental extension, which is that which separates from the body, and is not a part of it, but rather an accidental quantity. So we say: the contiguity that is removed by the occurrence of separation, if it is the first, then the receptive of separation would not be the body because of its annihilation at that time, thus your statement in proving matter—that the body is receptive to contiguity and separation—is invalid; and if it is the second, the body is not annihilated by its annihilation, so there is no obstacle to it being receptive to separation by itself without needing matter. It cannot be said that accidental extension is a necessary concomitant of corporeality, and so its removal would be by the removal of corporeality, because we say: what is removed is a particular extension, not a necessary concomitant, and the necessary concomitant is extension as such and is not removed, as is seen in a candle where extents change while corporeality remains the same. It cannot be said: likewise for essential extension, because we say this does not harm the intended point, rather it benefits it, because that from which a particular extension is removed and two others occur is the meaning of matter. Thus they said: just as extents change over a remaining substance which is form, so do forms change over a remaining substance which is matter. Rather, the answer: that the meaning of the body's reception of separation is not that it receives it while remaining with all its parts, but rather that in it there is a part remaining itself which is the true receptive of it and of the contiguity that is opposite to it. As for the absence of complete removal, it is due to the necessity of distinguishing between the annihilation of the water of a jar entirely and its separation into many waters. As for the absence of remaining with the complete essence and the restriction of removal to identity, it is due to the annihilation of the part which is this contiguity. The fair truth is that the separation of water into many waters is not an annihilation of a substance and the occurrence of another, and that what remains in both states is water in its reality, even if it changes in its identity—there is no part of it that is matter.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and its lengthy answer. The objection argues that if contiguity is a part of the body, then the body cannot be receptive to both contiguity and separation: if contiguity is a part, its removal would destroy the body, so the body cannot receive separation; conversely, if the body is receptive to separation, then contiguity cannot be a part. The answer begins by distinguishing two senses of contiguity: essential contiguity (the substantial extension that is a part of the body and inseparable) and accidental contiguity (a separable accidental quantity). The contiguity that vanishes upon separation is accidental, not essential. Thus, when a body divides, the accidental extension changes while the essential substrate (the body as a substance) remains. The answer then addresses potential objections using examples: a candle's size changes while its corporeality remains; similarly, the persisting substrate of change is matter (hayula). Finally, the author refines the notion: the body's reception of separation does not mean all parts remain; rather, a part (the substrate) persists while the contiguity-part perishes. Using the water-in-a-jar example, separation is not annihilation of substance but a change in identity, and the underlying reality (water) remains. The conclusion affirms that matter is not a part of the body's essence but the substratum of change.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Separation involves the annihilation of the original substance and creation of a new one.
Correction: Separation only changes the identity, not the substance; the same reality (like water) persists.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when water separates into multiple waters, what remains?
A) A new substance different from water
B) The same water in reality, with changed identity
C) Nothing remains
D) Only the contiguity-part remains

Key Takeaway
Separation is a change in identity, not substance; matter is the substratum of such change, not a component of the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that your identity can change while your essential reality remains. When you lose something you thought defined you — a role, a relationship, a part of your life — what deeper reality of yourself do you fear losing? How does this distinction between identity and essence challenge you to trust that your core being remains in Allah's hands?
?
The passage says matter is not a part of the water's essence. Consider: what 'parts' of your life have you mistaken for your essence — your possessions, your status, your achievements? Are you ready to see them as temporary accidents that can change without destroying who you are before Allah?
?
What would it mean for you to truly believe that your existence is not a fragile accident but a persistent substance upheld by the Creator? If your identity can shift without your essence being annihilated, what fear about death or loss is the passage calling you to surrender right now?
