Essential vs. Accidental Extension
Sanusi defines essential vs. accidental extension and begins the refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critical distinction between essential extension (which is part of the body) and accidental extension (which can separate without destroying the body).

Core Idea
Extension can be part of a body or an accidental quantity.

The author begins by defining two types of extension: essential and accidental. This distinction is crucial for the ensuing argument against the need for a hyle (prime matter) as a substrate for change.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Identify the two types of extension and their definitions.
Essential extension is inseparable; accidental extension can separate.
2
Understand the consequence
Notice how the author uses this to argue that if connection lost in separation were essential, the body would cease, so it can't be receptive.
This sets up the refutation.
3
Reflect on the logic
Consider why the author says the opponent's argument for hyle fails if the connection is accidental.
The body remains, so no need for hyle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو الجزء وليس بزايل عن الجسم ، بل ينعدم الجسم بانعدامه وعلى عرضي هو الزائل عن الجسم ، وليس بجزء له، بل كمية عارضة . من لأنا نقول : الاتصال الذي يزول بطريان الانفصال إن كان هو الأول لم يكن القابل للانفصال هو الجسم لانعدامه حينئذ ، فيبطل قولكم في إثبات الهيولي: أن الجسم قابل للاتصال والانفصال، وإن كان هو الثاني لم ينعدم الجسم بانعدامه، فلم يمتنع كونه قابلاً للانفصال بذاته من غير افتقار إلى الهيولي، لا يقال الامتداد العرضي من لوازم الجسمية ، فزواله بزوالها، لأنا نقول : الزائل امتداد مخصوص وليس بلازم واللازم امتداد ما وليس بزائل، كما يرى في الشمعة تبدل المقادير مع بقاء الجسمية بعينها، لا يقال فكذلك الامتداد الجوهري لأنا نقول هذا لا يضر بالمقصود ، بل يفيده لأن ما يزول عنه خصوص امتداد ويطرأ آخران هو المعنى بالهيولي ولذا قالوا : كما تتبدل المقادير على جوهر باق هو الصورة تتبدل الصورة على جوهر باق هو المادة، بل الجواب : أن ليس معنى قبول الجسم للانفصال أنه بقائه بجميع أجزائه يقبله، بل إن فيه جزءاً باقياً بعينه هو القابل بالحقيقة له وللاتصال الذي يقابله أما عدم الزوال بالكلية فلضرورة التفرقة بين انعدام ماء الجرة بالمرة وبين انفصاله إلى مياه جمة، وأما عدم البقاء بتمام الماهية واقتصار الزوال على الهوية فلانعدام الجزء الذي هو امتداد جوهري، والإنصاف أن انفصال الماء إلى المياه ليس بانعدام جوهر وحدوث آخر، وأن الباقي في الحالين هو الماء بحقيقته، وإن تبدل في هويته لا جزء منها هو الهيولي.
It is the part and is not something separable from the body; rather, the body ceases to exist with its cessation. And accidental is that which separates from the body and is not a part of it, but rather an accidental quantity. Because we say: The connection that ceases with the occurrence of separation—if it is the first, then the body would not be receptive to separation due to its non-existence at that time, so your statement in proving the hyle that the body is receptive to connection and separation is invalid. And if it is the second, the body does not cease to exist with its cessation, so it is not impossible for it to be receptive to separation by itself without needing the hyle. It cannot be said that accidental extension is a necessary concomitant of corporeality, so its cessation is with its cessation, because we say: What ceases is a specific extension, not a necessary concomitant, and what is necessary is some extension and it does not cease, as is seen in the candle where dimensions change while the corporeality remains the same. It cannot be said that likewise substantial extension, because we say: This does not harm the objective, rather it benefits it, because that from which a specific extension ceases and two others occur is what is meant by hyle. Therefore they said: Just as dimensions change upon a remaining substance which is the form, the form changes upon a remaining substance which is matter. Rather, the answer is: The meaning of the body's receptivity to separation is not that it accepts it while remaining with all its parts, but rather that it has a part that remains in itself which is the real recipient for it and for the connection that opposes it. As for non-cessation entirely, it is due to the necessary distinction between the complete annihilation of the water in a jar and its separation into many waters. As for non-remaining with the complete essence and the restriction of cessation to the identity, it is due to the cessation of the part that is substantial extension. The fair judgment is that the separation of water into waters is not the cessation of a substance and the occurrence of another, and that what remains in both states is water in its reality, even if its identity changes, none of its parts is the hyle.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection. The author first distinguishes between essential extension (which is part of the body and its cessation entails the body's cessation) and accidental extension (which is not a part and can separate without destroying the body). He then argues that the connection lost in separation cannot be the essential extension, because then the body would cease to exist and could not be receptive to separation—contradicting the proponent's claim that the body is receptive to both connection and separation. If it is accidental, then the body does not cease, so it could be receptive by itself without hyle. The opponent may object that accidental extension is a necessary concomitant of corporeality, so when the extension goes, the body goes. The author refutes this by noting that what ceases is a specific extension, not extension as a necessary concomitant; the necessary concomitant is some extension, which remains. He gives the analogy of a candle: dimensions change but the corporeality remains. The opponent may then say that substantial extension is similar, but the author replies that this actually proves his point: what remains after the change of specific extensions is precisely what is meant by hyle. He then clarifies that the body's receptivity to separation does not require that it remain with all its parts; rather, there is a permanent part that is the real substrate. He distinguishes between total annihilation and mere separation, and concludes that in the separation of water, the water remains in reality even though its identity changes.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Extension is a simple property that defines a body.
Correction: Extension is of two types: essential (part of the body's substance) and accidental (a quantity that can change).

Checkpoint
According to the author, what happens to the body when an accidental extension is removed?
A) The body ceases to exist.
B) The body remains with a different extension.
C) The body loses its corporeality.
D) The body becomes receptive to separation.

Key Takeaway
Essential extension is integral to the body; accidental extension can change without destroying the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes treat temporary attributes of yourself or your surroundings as if they are essential—like youth, health, or wealth—forgetting that your core being depends solely on God? How can the distinction between essential and accidental extension help you detach from what is transient?
?
The candle retains its corporeality even as its shape changes. What in your life must 'stay the same' for you to feel secure? Is that thing truly essential, or is it an accidental extension you've mistaken for necessary?
?
The author refutes the need for a substrate (hyle) by showing that bodies are self-sufficient in change. What does this say about your reliance on created intermediaries versus direct dependence on the Creator? Are you searching for a 'hyle' to ground your identity when only Allah is the real Sustainer?

Refuting the Need for Hyle
Sanusi uses the candle analogy to refute hyle and gives the water example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the candle analogy refutes the necessity of a hyle (prime matter) as a substrate for change.

Core Idea
Change of dimensions does not require a separate substrate; the body itself persists.

After distinguishing extension types, the author uses the example of a candle to show that dimensions change while corporeality remains. This undermines the argument for hyle as an underlying matter that remains through change.

Guided Path
1
Read the candle example
Understand that the candle's size changes but its corporeality remains.
This shows accidental extension changes.
2
Follow the opponent's objection
The opponent argues that substantial extension also changes, so hyle is needed.
The author refutes this.
3
Understand the author's final answer
The meaning of receptivity is that a part remains; separation is not annihilation.
The water example clarifies.
4
Grasp the fair judgment
Separation of water into waters is not a change of substance; water remains.
No hyle is needed.

Source Text

The Fifth Objection: The Problem of Material in Separated Bodies and Its Answer
The author presents and refutes an objection concerning the matter of separated bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection against the existence of hyle (matter) regarding the separation of one body into two, and the answer that matter is a mere disposition whose unity or multiplicity depends on form.

Core Idea
Matter is not a real thing that becomes many; unity and multiplicity come from form.

In this passage, the author tackles a logical puzzle: when a single body splits into two, what happens to its underlying matter? The objection tries to show that the concept of a single substrate leads to absurdity. The author's answer reveals a deeper understanding of matter as pure potentiality, not a substance that can be one or many in itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the structure of the dilemma: two main horns with sub-branches.
2
Understand the dilemma: same matter vs. different matter
The objection presents two possibilities: either the matter of the two resulting bodies is identical or it is different. Each leads to an absurdity. Identify the two horns and the sub-cases under 'different'.
The first horn (same matter) is dismissed quickly as obviously false.
3
Explore the implications of each horn
If the matter is different, the objection considers whether the two matters were present before separation or not. Both lead to conclusions that contradict the proponent's claim. Work through the reasoning for each sub-case.
Note the argument that if both matters existed before, the body would have infinite parts; if not, separation would be annihilation.
4
Examine the answer: matter as disposition dependent on form
The author responds that both matters existed but were united by the connection. Multiplicity after separation does not entail annihilation because matter is mere disposition, not a real continuum. Its unity or multiplicity is attributed in dependence on the form.
This answer resolves the dilemma by reinterpreting what matter is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أن الجسم الواحد إذا انفصل إلى جسمين، فإما أن تكون مادة هذا مادة ذاك بعينها، وهو مع كونه ضروري البطلان يستلزم أن يكون الواحد بالشخص في حيزين ومتصفاً بجسميتين، وإما غيرها وحينئذ إما أن تكون المادتان قد كانتا موجودتين عند الاتصال فيشمل الجسم على أجزاء بالفعل، بل يكون له مواد غير متناهية بحسب قبول الانقسام، بل يتألف من أجسام لا تتناهى ضرورة أن كل مادة تستدعي صورة على حدة، أو غير موجودتين، فيلزم أن يكون انفصال انعداماً له بالكلية لا بمجرد صورته الاتصالية، وهو مع بطلانه يبطل مقصود الاستدلال أعني بقاء أمر قابل للاتصال والانفصال. وجوابه : أن المادتين كانتا موجودتين لكن بصفة الواحدة لوحدة الاتصال. والآن بصفة التعدد لتعدده ولا يلزم من تعددها بعد الوحدة انعدامها وافتقارها إلى مادة أخرى لما سبق من أنها استعداد محض ليس بمتصل واحد في نفسه، كما ليس بمتعدد، وإنما يفرض له ذلك ببقاء تبعاً للصورة، فمناظر هذا الإشكال وإن أطنب فيه الإمام الرازي راجع إلى الثالث
The fifth: That when one body separates into two bodies, either the matter of this one is the matter of that one itself—and besides being obviously false, it entails that the individual is in two places and characterized by two corporealities—or it is different. Then either the two matters were existing at the time of connection, so the body contains parts actually, rather it has infinite matters according to the capacity for division, indeed it is composed of infinite bodies necessarily because every matter requires a distinct form—or they were not existing, then it would follow that the separation is a complete annihilation of it, not merely of its connecting form, and along with its falsity, it invalidates the purpose of the argument, namely the remaining of something receptive to connection and separation. Its answer: That the two matters were existing but with the attribute of unity due to the unity of connection, and now with the attribute of multiplicity due to its multiplicity. And their multiplicity after unity does not entail their annihilation and need for another matter, due to what preceded: that it is mere disposition, not a single continuum in itself, nor is it multiple, but it is considered as such by remaining in dependence on the form. So the counterpart of this objection, even though Imam al-Razi elaborated on it, goes back to the third.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection against the existence of hyle. The objection poses a dilemma: when one body separates into two, either the matter of the two resultant bodies is the same or different. If the same, that implies the individual is in two places with two corporealities, which is absurd. If different, then either both matters were present before separation (implying the original body had actual parts infinite in number) or they were not present (implying separation is annihilation of the body, not a change of form). Both alternatives contradict the proponent's claim that a substrate remains. The author gives the answer: both matters existed but were united in the state of connection, and now they are multiple. Their multiplicity after unity does not entail annihilation because matter is mere disposition, not a real continuum. It is only considered one or many in dependence on the form. The author notes that this objection, though elaborated by al-Razi, reduces to the third objection.

Key Terms
مادة
صورة
اتصال
انفصال
استعداد
الواحد بالشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that matter cannot remain when a body separates; therefore hyle does not exist.
Correction: The answer shows that matter is a mere disposition (استعداد محض) that can be one when united by form and many when separated. Its multiplicity does not entail annihilation because matter is not a real continuum.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given in this passage, why does the multiplicity of matters after separation not require their annihilation?
A) Because matters are eternal and indestructible.
B) Because matter is a mere disposition that is considered one or many only in dependence on the form.
C) Because the body is composed of atoms that cannot be annihilated.
D) Because matters only exist in potentiality and not in actuality.

Key Takeaway
Matter is mere disposition (استعداد محض); it is not intrinsically one or many—its unity or multiplicity is attributed to it in dependence on form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your sense of spiritual identity depend more on external unity (like being part of a community) than on your intrinsic disposition before Allah? How would it feel if that external unity were broken—would you still know who you are?
?
When you experience a 'separation' in your life—such as leaving a group or losing a role—do you believe your true self (your soul) remains intact? Or do you feel annihilated because your identity was tied to that connection?
?
You just learned that matter is mere disposition, not a real substance. What if your own worth is not a fixed substance either, but a disposition that Allah observes and values? Would you be willing to let go of the need to be 'one' or 'many' in the eyes of others, and simply rest in being His servant?

intermediate
4 min
Objection to Hyle Proof for Celestial Bodies
The author raises an objection against the universal proof for hyle, targeting bodies that cannot actually separate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the proof for hyle fails for bodies like the celestial sphere that cannot actually separate, and why imaginary division does not necessitate an external substrate.

Core Idea
Actual separation is required for the hyle proof; imaginary division does not demand a real substrate.

The author continues examining the proof for hyle (prime matter) in all bodies. He raises a challenging objection: some bodies, like the celestial sphere, cannot undergo actual separation. Their divisibility is only imaginary. This passage presents that objection and notes that it will be answered later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the objection clearly.
Notice the terms used: انفصال انفكاكي and انقسام وهمي.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The objection is that the proof requires actual separation, but some bodies (like the celestial sphere) cannot actually separate. Their division is only imaginary, which does not prove an external substrate.
3
Reflect on the implication and note the promised answer
Consider why imaginary division is insufficient. Also note that the author promises an answer in the later section on the branches of hyle.
This objection shows that the proof is not universal.

وههنا إشكال آخر، وهو أن المطلوب ثبوت المادة لكل جسم، وهذا الدليل لا يتم في الجسم الذي يمتنع عليه الانفصال الانفكاكي، كالفلك . إذ قبول الانقسام الوهمي لا يستدعي قابلاً في الخارج، وسيجيء جوابه في فروع الهيولي.

This passage presents a further objection against the universal proof for hyle. The objection points out that the proof relies on actual separation (انفصال انفكاكي) to show the need for a substrate. However, some bodies, such as the celestial sphere (فلك), cannot undergo actual separation—they are indivisible in practice. Their divisibility is only imaginary. Imaginary division does not require an actual external substrate. The author notes that this objection will be answered later in the section on the branches of hyle (فروع الهيولي).

First Objection to the Proof of Hyle: Extension as Accident
First objection to the proof of hyle and the response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the proof of hyle and how the author argues that extension and continuity are accidents, not substances.

Core Idea
Extension and continuity are accidents, not substances.

In this passage, the author presents and refutes the first objection to the proof of prime matter (hyle). The objector attempts to show that extension and continuity are substances distinct from the body, but the author argues they are merely accidents inherent in the body. Understanding this objection clarifies the nature of physical composition and the role of accidents in defining substance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection's two interpretations
The objector presents two ways to interpret 'extension' and 'continuity'. First, if they mean the substance extended in dimensions, then they are the body itself. Second, if they mean what ordinary people understand, then they are accidents. Note how this sets up the refutation.
The objector is trying to show that extension cannot be a substance distinct from the body.
2
Examine the candle example
The objector uses the example of a candle whose extension persists through changes in shape and size. The author counters that this persisting extension is merely the absolute extension that remains through particular changes, with no underlying entity. This is analogous to shape persisting while being an accident.
The author denies any fixed, unchanging extension underlying the candle.
3
Follow the author's response
The author defines continuity as the accident of parts being connected (opposed to separation). When the candle splits, continuity ceases into two continuities, and the body becomes two bodies. Continuity is thus an accident, not a substance.
The response emphasizes that continuity is inherent in the body and cannot subsist independently.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The author concludes that extension in the sense of a self-subsisting substance extended in dimensions is not distinct from the body. Expressions like 'connective identity' do not prove otherwise. The objection fails to establish hyle as a substance.
The key point: extension and continuity do not imply a separate substratum; they are accidents of the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: أنه إن أريد بالامتداد والاتصال الجوهر الممتد في الجهات القابل للأبعاد، فلا نسلم أنه غير الجسم بالامتداد والاتصال، وإن أريد ما يفهمه العقلاء من هذين اللفظين، فلا نسلم أنه جوهر بل عرض، ودعوى كونه جزءاً من حقيقة الجسم، وأول ما يدرك من جوهريته غير مسموعة، والتمسك بأن في الشمعة امتداداً باقياً مع تبدل المقادير عليه ضعيف، لأن ذلك هو مطلق الامتداد الباقي بتعاقب خصوصياته، من غير ثبوت أمر سوى الخصوصيات، كما يقطع ببقاء الشكل عند تبدل الأشكال مع القطع بأنه عارض. وبالجملة، فلا نسلم أن فيها امتداداً معيناً ثابتاً لا يتغير أصلاً. فإن قيل : نعني به ذلك الأمر الذي لم ينعدم عند تبدل الأشكال والمقادير وانعدم عند انفصال الشمعة إلى الشمعتين. قلنا هو ما يقابل الانفصال من اتصال الأجزاء المفروضة بعضها بالبعض وهو عرض والباقي هو الجسم نفسه. وحاصل الكلام، أنا لا نسلم أن الاتصال والامتداد بالمعنى الذي يقابل الانفصال، ويزول بطريانه جوهر وجزء من الجسم، بل لا يعقل منه إلا أمر لا قوام له بنفسه، ولا غنى له عن الموضوع، فلا يكون إلا عرضاً، غايته أنه لازم للجسم، فعند زواله إلى اتصالين، يصير الجسم جسمين، حتى لو أمكن زواله لا إلى اتصالين، انعدم الجسم بالكلية، وأما بمعنى الأمر الذي شأنه الامتداد في الجهات وصحة فرض الأبعاد فلا نسلم أنه غير الجسم كيف ولا يعقل منه إلا أمر قائم بنفسه، متميز بذاته مستغن في قوامه عن المحل؟ والتعبير عنه بالهوية الاتصالية أو المتصل بالذات، أو نحو ذلك من العبارات لا يفيد.
First: If by extension and continuity is meant the substance extended in dimensions, receptive of dimensions, then we do not concede that it is other than the body in extension and continuity. And if is meant what the rational people understand from these two expressions, then we do not concede that it is a substance; rather it is an accident. The claim that it is part of the reality of the body, and that it is the first thing perceived of its substantiality, is not accepted. And the clinging to the fact that in the candle there is an extension remaining with the change of measures upon it is weak, because that is the absolute extension remaining with the succession of its particularities, without the establishment of anything besides the particularities, just as one is certain of the persistence of shape upon the change of shapes while being certain that it is an accident. In sum, we do not concede that in it there is a specific, fixed extension that does not change at all. So if it is said: We mean by it that thing which did not cease upon the change of shapes and measures, and ceased when the candle split into two candles. We say: It is what opposes separation, namely the continuity of the supposed parts with each other, and it is an accident, and what remains is the body itself. The upshot of the discourse is that we do not concede that continuity and extension, in the sense that opposes separation and ceases by its occurrence, is a substance and a part of the body. Rather, nothing is understood from it except something that subsists not by itself and has no independence from a subject, so it is nothing but an accident, its utmost being that it is necessary for the body. So when it ceases into two continuities, the body becomes two bodies; even if its cessation without two continuities were possible, the body would cease entirely. As for in the sense of the thing whose property is extension in dimensions and the validity of supposing dimensions, we do not concede that it is other than the body; how could it be, since nothing is understood from it except something self-subsisting, distinguished by itself, independent in its constitution from a locus? And expression about it as 'connective identity' or 'essentially continuous,' or similar expressions, is not useful.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (اعتراض ورد) regarding the proof for hyle (prime matter). The objector presents the first of several objections. He argues that if by 'extension' and 'continuity' one means the substance extended in dimensions, then it is not distinct from the body—it is the body itself. If one means what ordinary people understand, then it is an accident, not a substance. The claim that extension is a part of the body's essence or that it is the first perceived aspect of substantiality is rejected. The example of a candle's extension persisting through changes of shape is countered: that persisting extension is merely the absolute extension that remains through particular changes, with no underlying entity apart from the particulars; similarly, shape persists through change of shapes but is an accident. The objector concludes that no fixed, unchanging extension exists. The author then gives the response: 'if it is said... we say.' The response defines continuity as the accident of parts being connected (opposed to separation), and it is an accident; the body itself remains. The author elaborates that continuity and extension in the sense of 'opposed to separation' are not substances but accidents necessarily inherent in the body; when they cease into two continuities, the body becomes two bodies, or if it ceases without two continuities, the body perishes. As for extension in the sense of a self-subsisting substance extended in dimensions, the author denies that it is distinct from the body—nothing is understood except a self-subsisting entity, and expressions like 'connective identity' do not help.

Key Terms
امتداد
اتصال
جوهر
عرض
هيولي
موضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Extension and continuity are substances that underlie bodies and persist through change.
Correction: The author demonstrates that extension and continuity are accidents, not substances. They do not persist independently; they are inherent in the body and change with it.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the first objection, what is the continuity that opposes separation?
A) A substance that remains when the candle splits.
B) An accident of parts being connected to each other.
C) The body itself.
D) A necessary attribute of prime matter.

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because continuity and extension, when properly understood, are accidents of the body, not independent substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own body feels continuous and extended. If that continuity is merely an accident dependent on Allah's preservation, how does that change the way you view your physical existence? What can you do today to remind yourself that every moment of your 'continuity' is contingent upon Him?
?
The objector clung to the idea of a fixed extension, but the responder showed it is only an accident that changes. When you look at your accomplishments, relationships, or even your faith, do you see them as fixed substances or as accidents that Allah sustains? What would it mean for you to live with the constant awareness that nothing in your life has independent stability?

Third Objection: Two Extensions and Their Rebuttal
Presents the third objection and its rebuttal concerning two extensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against hylomorphism and how the author defends the coexistence of substantial and accidental extensions.

Core Idea
Two extensions in one body are distinct in reality even if indistinguishable in sense.

In this passage, the author presents the third objection to the theory of hylomorphism. The objector argues that positing two extensions in a single body leads to absurdity—either difference without a substrate or indistinguishability. The rebuttal clarifies that the two extensions are distinct in reality and have different loci. Let's examine the argument and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the objection and rebuttal unfold naturally.
Notice the two conditional scenarios the objector constructs.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objector presents two possibilities: if one extension exceeds the other, the excess would lack a substrate; if they are equal, they would be indistinguishable and duality collapses.
These are classic dilemmas in kalām arguments.
3
Understand the rebuttal
The author responds that the two extensions are distinct in reality (bi-l-ḥaqīqa) because they have different loci: the accidental extension inheres in the substantial extension or body, while the substantial extension inheres in matter. If the objector means sensory indistinguishability, that is not a problem.
Distinguishing between reality and sensory perception is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أنه لو كان في الجسم امتدادان أحدهما جوهري، والآخر عرضي، فإن فضل أحدهما على الآخر وقع القدر الذي به التفاضل لا في مادة وهو محال ، إذ لا عرض بدون الموضوع ولا صورة بدون الهيولي. وبالجملة لا حال بدون المحل، وإن لم يفضل بل تساويا في جميع الأقطار ارتفع الامتياز والأثنينية ، لأن امتياز أفراد الطبيعة الواحدة الحالة إنما يكون بحسب المحل، وهذا مدفوع بأنهما متميزان بالحقيقة مع أن محل العرضي هو الجوهري أو الجسم، ومحل الجوهري هو المادة وإن أريد عدم الامتياز في الحس فلا ضير.
Third: That if there were in the body two extensions, one substantial and the other accidental, then if one exceeds the other, the amount by which they differ would occur not in a material — and that is impossible, for there is no accident without a subject and no form without hyle. In sum, there is no state without a locus. And if they do not exceed but are equal in all dimensions, then distinction and duality would be eliminated, because the distinction of individuals of a single inherent nature is only by the locus. And this is rebutted by the fact that they are distinct in reality, while the locus of the accidental is the substantial or the body, and the locus of the substantial is the matter; and if the absence of distinction in the senses is intended, then no harm.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage contains the third objection (الثالث) and its answer. The objector posits that if there were two extensions in a body—one substantial (the supposed hyle) and one accidental—then if one exceeds the other, the excess would have to occur without a substrate, which is impossible because accidents need a subject and forms need hyle. In short, no state (حال) exists without a locus (محل). If they are equal in all dimensions, then they would be indistinguishable and duality would vanish, since individuals of a single inherent nature are distinguished only by their locus. The rebuttal (وهذا مدفوع) responds: they are distinct in reality (بالحقيقة); the locus of the accidental extension is the substantial extension (or the body), and the locus of the substantial extension is the matter (المادة). If one means that they are not distinct in sensory perception, then that is not problematic (لا ضير).

Key Terms

امتداد عرضي
محل
هيولي
تفاضل
امتياز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if two extensions existed in a single body, they would require a third substrate to account for any difference between them.
Correction: The author replies that the two extensions are already distinct in reality because they have different loci: the accidental extension inheres in the substantial extension or body, and the substantial extension inheres in matter. No third substrate is needed.

Checkpoint
How does the author rebut the third objection's argument that two extensions would lack distinction if equal?
A) By denying two extensions exist.
B) By saying they are distinct in reality and differ in their loci.
C) By claiming extension is not a nature.
D) By appealing to sensory evidence.

Key Takeaway
The existence of two extensions in a body is coherent because they are distinct in reality through their different loci; sensory indistinguishability is irrelevant.

What will you do the next time you are tempted to rely only on your senses to assess truth?
?
The objector assumes that if something is not sensed as distinct, it must be identical. How does this passage urge you to reconsider the priority of reason and revelation over sensory experience?
?

In what areas of your life are you living as if things can exist independently without relying on Allah? What is one step you can take today to affirm your total dependence on Him?

Argument Against Hyle and Its Answer
Argument denying hyle's existence with a dilemma and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a kalam argument against the existence of hyle (prime matter) based on the dilemma of extension, and how the answer begins to refute this argument.

Core Idea
The argument against hyle relies on a dilemma about extension; its answer challenges the assumption that dependent existence implies inherence.

This passage presents a proof (دليل) against the existence of hyle, the prime matter posited by philosophers. The proof sets up a dilemma: if hyle is not extended, it cannot serve as a locus for extended things; if it is extended, it must be either independent or dependent. Each horn leads to problems. The answer then begins to show a flaw in this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once, noting the sentence structure and the logical connectors.
Pay attention to the conditional 'إن' and the disjunction 'إما... وإما'.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find the two possibilities: non-extended (if) and extended (then). Then note the sub-branches under extended: independent vs dependent.
Each branch leads to a different problematic consequence.
3
Understand the answer's opening
Read the brief answer: 'لا يلزم أن يكون بحلولها بل...' and consider what assumption it challenges.
The answer rejects the implication that dependent extension equals inherence.
4
Reflect on the theological context
Think about why kalam scholars would want to deny hyle. This argument is part of a larger debate about the nature of material substance.
Hyle is a key concept in Aristotelian physics, but kalam rejects it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وقد يستدل) على نفي الهيولي بأنها إن لم تتحيز لم تصلح محلاً لما له اختصاص بالحيز، وإن تحيزت فإما بالاستقلال فكان مثل الجسمية، فلم يجامعها، ولم يكن بالمحلية أولى ولزم استغناء الجسمية عن المادة أو تسلسل المواد. وإما بالتبعية، فكانت صفة للجسمية حالة فيها . ويجاب بأن عدم الاستقلال لا يلزم أن يكون بحلولها بل
He said: And it may be argued for the negation of hyle: that if it is not extended (lit. not occupying space), it is not fit to be a locus for what has special relation to space; and if it is extended, then either independently, so it would be like corporeality, so it would not combine with it, and it would not be more entitled to be a locus, and necessarily corporeality would be independent of matter, or there would be a regress of matters; or [if it is extended] dependently, so it would be an attribute of corporeality inhering in it. And it is answered that independence does not necessarily entail that it is by inherence, but...
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (دليل) against the existence of hyle (prime matter) and the beginning of its answer. The proof is a dilemma: either hyle is not extended (non-spatial) or it is extended. If non-extended, it cannot serve as a locus for something that occupies space. If extended, then either its extension is independent (like a substance) — then it would be like corporeality itself, so it would not combine with corporeality, nor would it have priority as a locus, and either corporeality would be independent of matter or there would be an infinite regress of matters. Or its extension is dependent (like an accident) — then it would be an attribute of corporeality inhering in it. The answer begins: 'lack of independence does not necessarily entail that it is by inherence (حلول) but...' The response is cut off, but it likely continues to argue that hyle can be extended dependently without being an accident in the usual sense.

Key Terms
نفي الهيولي
تحيز
استقلال
تبعية
جسمية
حلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dependent existence always implies inherence as an accident.
Correction: The answer argues that lack of independent extension does not necessarily entail inherence (حلول); hyle could have dependent existence without being an accident in the usual sense.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of the dilemma against hyle?
A) If hyle is non-extended, it cannot be a locus for extended things.
B) If hyle is extended, it must be a body.
C) If hyle is independent, it is a substance.
D) If hyle is dependent, it is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The proof against hyle uses a dilemma about extension, but the answer challenges the assumption that dependent existence equals inherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument forces us to categorize hyle as either independent or dependent—a binary that mirrors how we see ourselves. Where in your life do you assume that depending on someone or something means being less real, less valuable? How does God's answer to this dilemma (that dependence does not equal inherence) challenge that assumption? This week, can you identify one area where you can lean on God without fearing that makes you weak?
?
The proof against hyle says that if it were not extended, it couldn't be a locus for extended things. Yet the answer suggests a different kind of relationship. Think about your relationship with Allah: do you ever feel that because you are limited (like a non-extended thing) you cannot be a container for His attributes or guidance? What does the answer in this lesson say about the possibility of holding something far greater than oneself without being independent? Will you, today, ask Allah to make you a locus for His mercy despite your limitations?

Agreement on the Unity of the Nature of Parts between the Two Schools
States that both schools agree parts are identical in essence; difference in bodies is by accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common ground between Ash'ari mutakallimun and philosophers on the nature of atoms and the source of diversity in bodies.

Core Idea
Both schools agree: parts are identical in essence; difference comes from accidents.

This passage highlights a surprising point of agreement between two otherwise opposing schools of atomism. Before diving into their differences, Sanusi shows that they both accept that the fundamental parts of bodies share the same nature, and that the variety we observe is due to accidents. He then outlines their different explanations for those accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two groups
The passage refers to 'the two groups' (al-ṭā’ifatayn): those who say parts are indivisible (mutakallimūn) and those who say they are divisible in estimation (philosophers). Read carefully to see where they agree.
The agreement is on the nature of the parts, not on their divisibility.
2
Grasp the point of agreement
Both groups agree that the parts (ajzā’) are similar (mutamāthila) in all bodies, meaning their substance is one by nature. Bodies differ only by accidents, not by essence.
3
Understand the different causal explanations
The passage then gives two different accounts for why accidents vary: for the Ash‘arīs, it is due to God’s free choice; for the philosophers, it is due to different shapes of the parts.
This difference is the main divergence between the schools regarding bodies.
4
Consider the raised question
The passage ends with a question: if some accidents become essential to bodies, then wouldn't bodies differ in essence after all? This is left for future discussion.
This shows that the issue is not fully settled even within the agreed framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ثم المشهور من الطائفتين أن طبيعة الأجزاء واحدة في جميع الأجسام فيكون اختلافها بحسب الأعراض، ويستند اختلاف الأعراض عندنا إلى قدرة المختار، وعندهم إلى اختلاف الأشكال فلا حاجة إلى جعل بعض الأعراض داخلة في حقيقة الجسم . أي القائلين بكون الجسم من أجزاء لا تنقسم أصلاً، والقائلين بأنها تنقسم وهماً لا فعلاً إنها متماثلة أي جوهرها واحد بالطبع في جميع الأجسام. فاختلاف الأجسام إنما يكون بحسب الأعراض دون الماهيات واختلاف الأعراض مستند عند المتكلمين إلى الفاعل المختار وعند الآخرين إلى اختلاف أشكال الأجزاء على ما صرح به في الشفاء، وهل يلزم أن يكون بعض تلك الأعراض داخلاً في حقيقة الجسم، فتكون عوارض للأجزاء وذاتيات للأجسام فيتحقق اختلافها بحسب الماهية ؟ فيه كلام كما سيجيء إن شاء الله
He said: 'Then the well-known [view] of the two groups is that the nature of the parts is one in all bodies, so their difference is according to accidents. And the difference of accidents is based, according to us (the Ash'aris), on the power of the Choser (al-Mukhtar), and according to them (the philosophers) on the difference of shapes. So there is no need to make some of those accidents enter into the reality of the body.' That is: both those who say the body is composed of parts that do not divide at all and those who say they divide in estimation but not actually [agree] that they are similar, i.e., their substance (jawhar) is one by nature in all bodies. So the difference of bodies is only according to accidents, not essences (mahiyyat). And the difference of accidents is based, according to the mutakallimun, on the agent who chooses (al-fa'il al-mukhtar), and according to the others on the difference of the shapes of the parts, as stated in al-Shifa. And [the question]: does it necessarily follow that some of those accidents are internal to the reality of the body, such that they are accidents for the parts but essences (dhatiyyat) for the bodies, so that their difference occurs according to essence? There is discussion about that, as will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage explains the common ground between the two main schools of atomism: those who say atoms are indivisible (the mutakallimun) and those who say they are divisible in estimation (the philosophers). Both agree that the essence of the atoms is identical in all bodies; the diversity of bodies arises solely from accidents (a'rad). However, the cause of these accidents differs: the Ash'ari mutakallimun attribute them to the free choice of God (al-qadira al-mukhtar), while the philosophers attribute them to the different shapes of the atoms, as mentioned in Ibn Sina's al-Shifa. The passage then raises a further question: if some accidents are essential to bodies (like the accident of being a certain shape), then those accidents would be intrinsic to the body's reality, making bodies differ in essence rather than merely in accidents. The author says this issue will be discussed later.

Key Terms
الطائفتين
الأعراض
المختار
الأشكال
الماهية
الذاتيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two schools agree on the cause of diversity of bodies (both attribute it to accidents in the same way).
Correction: They only agree that parts are identical and diversity comes from accidents. They disagree on the cause of accidents: God's choice vs. shapes of parts.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what do both groups (the two schools) agree on regarding the parts of bodies?
A) They are different in shape.
B) They are one in nature (jawhar) in all bodies.
C) They are composed of five types.

Key Takeaway
The two schools of atomism agree that atoms are identical in essence across all bodies, but they differ on whether the diversity of accidents is caused by God's free choice or by the shapes of the atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the vast diversity of creation—different bodies, different forms—do you genuinely see it as a manifestation of God's free choice and wisdom, or do you subconsciously attribute it to impersonal natural causes? How does your honest answer shape your gratitude and reliance on Him?
?
If all atoms are essentially identical and differences are merely accidental, what does that say about the things you attach your identity to—your appearance, your possessions, your status? Are they truly what define you, or are they just accidents that could change in an instant by God's will?
?
Do you live as if the differences between people—their strengths, weaknesses, circumstances—are ultimately caused by God's perfect choice, or do you find yourself resenting the 'shapes' of your life? How would your heart change if you truly believed that every accidental difference is assigned by al-Mukhtar for a wise purpose?

The Agreement on the Unity of Parts and Role of Accidents
The author explains the common view on the similarity of parts and the role of accidents, with two explanations and a question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common ground between mutakallimun and philosophers on the composition of bodies, and how they differ in explaining the source of accidental differences.

Core Idea
Both groups agree that parts are identical; differences come from accidents, not essences.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid highlights a rare point of agreement between the mutakallimun and the philosophers. Despite their deep differences on the nature of parts (whether truly indivisible or only conceptually), they both hold that the substance of parts is identical across bodies. The source of variety in bodies is attributed entirely to accidents. The author then clarifies how each camp explains the origin of those accidents, and raises a critical question about whether some accidents might actually be essential to bodies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common view
Look for the phrase 'أن طبيعة الأجزاء واحدة' (the nature of the parts is one). Both groups agree that parts are similar in essence.
2
Understand the two explanations
Notice that the mutakallimun attribute accidental differences to God's power (قدرة المختار), while the philosophers attribute them to the shapes of the parts (اختلاف الأشكال).
This shows a fundamental difference in causality.
3
Consider the question raised
The author asks whether some accidents are internal to the reality of the body, hinting at a deeper discussion to come. Reflect on how this question challenges the idea that all accidents are purely accidental.
This connects to later discussions on essence and accident.

قال ثم المشهور من الطائفتين أن طبيعة الأجزاء واحدة في جميع الأجسام فيكون اختلافها بحسب الأعراض، ويستند اختلاف الأعراض عندنا إلى قدرة المختار، وعندهم إلى اختلاف الأشكال فلا حاجة إلى جعل بعض الأعراض داخلة في حقيقة الجسم. أي القائلين بكون الجسم من أجزاء لا تنقسم أصلاً، والقائلين بأنها تنقسم وهماً لا فعلاً إنها متماثلة أي جوهرها واحد بالطبع في جميع الأجسام. فاختلاف الأجسام إنما يكون بحسب الأعراض دون الماهيات واختلاف الأعراض مستند عند المتكلمين إلى الفاعل المختار وعند الآخرين إلى اختلاف أشكال الأجزاء على ما صرح به في الشفاء، وهل يلزم أن يكون بعض تلك الأعراض داخلاً في حقيقة الجسم، فتكون عوارض للأجزاء وذاتيات للأجسام فيتحقق اختلافها بحسب الماهية ؟ فيه كلام كما سيجيء إن شاء الله.
He said: Then what is well-known from the two groups is that the nature of the parts is one in all bodies, so their difference is according to accidents. There is discussion about it as will come, God willing.

This passage clarifies the agreement between the two groups (the atomists and those who consider parts divisible in imagination) that the fundamental parts are similar, and differences in bodies are due to accidents. The mutakallimun attribute the differences in accidents to God's will, while the philosophers attribute them to the shapes of the parts. The author then raises a question: does this require that some accidents become part of the essence of body? He says there is discussion to come.

Every Body Requires Matter
First branch: every body requires matter due to corporeality's specific nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every body, including celestial spheres, requires matter according to the argument that corporeality is a specific nature with a universal necessary concomitant.

Core Idea
Corporeality is a specific nature, not a genus, so its need for matter is universal.

This passage presents the first branch of the view that bodies are composed of matter (hayula) and form (sura). It argues that every body, even celestial spheres that do not actually divide, must have matter because corporeality itself is a specific nature whose essential concomitant is dependence on matter. The author then verifies this by distinguishing corporeality from magnitude, which is not determinate without differentiate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the full English translation. Notice the logical flow from the claim to the proof.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that every body (including those not capable of disjunctive division, like celestial spheres) is composed of matter. Find where this is stated.
It is the first sentence after the enumeration.
3
Understand the proof
The proof is that corporeality is a specific nature, not a genus. It does not differ by differentiate but only by accidents. Since in divisible bodies corporeality requires matter due to its essence, the same must hold for all bodies because the necessary concomitant of an essence does not differ.
This is a key kalam reasoning pattern.
4
Examine the verification from al-Shifa
The passage contrasts corporeality with magnitude. Corporeality is determinate in itself; magnitude requires differentiate (like line or surface) to be determinate. This shows that corporeality is a specific nature, reinforcing the universality of its need for matter.
Understanding this contrast clarifies the argument.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does this argument matter for kalam cosmology? It establishes that even the heavenly bodies are composed of matter, countering views that they are simple or purely spiritual.
This connects to broader theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر من فروع القول بتركب الجسم من الهيولي والصورة خمسة : الأول: ثبوت ذلك لكل جسم، وإن لم يكن قابلاً للانفصال الانفكاكي كالفلكيات وذلك لأن الجسمية. أعني الامتداد الجوهري طبيعة نوعية، إذ لا تختلف حيث تختلف إلا بالعوارض والمشخصات دون الفصول، وقد ثبت أنها فيما يقبل الانفصال الانفكاكي مفتقرة إلى المادة، نظراً إلى ذاتها الاتصالية من غير اعتبار بالتشخصات، والأسباب الخارجة، فكذا فيما لا يقبله، لأن لازم الماهية لا يختلف، ولا يتخلف وتحقيق ذلك ما ذكر في الشفاء إن جسمية إذا خالفت جسمية أخرى تكون لأجل أن هذه حارة، وتلك باردة، وهذه لها طبيعة فلكية، وهذه لها طبيعة عنصرية، وهي أمور لا تلحق الجسمية من خارج، فإن الجسمية في الخارج موجودة، والطبيعة الفلكية موجودة أخرى، وقد انضاف إلى تلك الطبيعة القائمة المشار إليها هذه الطبيعة الأخرى في الخارج، بخلاف المقدار الذي هو ليس في نفسه شيئاً محصلاً ما لم يتنوع بأن يكون خطاً أو سطحاً إذ ليست المقدارية موجودة، والخطية موجودة أخرى، بل الخطية نفسها هي المحمولة عليها، فالجسمية مع كل شيء يفرض بشيء متقرر هو جسمية فقط من غير زيادة، وأما المقدار فلا مقدار فقط بل لا بد من فصول حتى يوجد ذاتاً متقررة إما خطاً أو سطحاً.
He mentioned from the branches of the statement that the body is composed of matter (hayula) and form (sura) five: the first: the affirmation of that for every body, even if it is not capable of disjunctive division, like the celestial spheres. That is because corporeality (al-jismiyya), meaning substantial extension, is a specific nature (tabi'a naw'iyya), since it does not differ where it differs except by accidents and individuators, not by differentiate. And it has been established that it, in what accepts disjunctive division, is in need of matter, with regard to its continuous essence without consideration of individuations and external causes. So likewise in what does not accept it, because the necessary concomitant of the essence does not differ nor fail. And the verification of that is what was mentioned in al-Shifa: that if one corporeality differs from another corporeality, it is because this one is hot and that one is cold, and this one has a celestial nature, and that one has an elemental nature, and these are matters that do not attach to corporeality from outside. For corporeality exists externally, and the celestial nature is another existence, and that other nature has been added to that indicated subsisting nature externally, unlike magnitude (al-miqdar), which is not in itself something determinate unless it is diversified by being a line or a surface. Since magnitude-as-such does not exist, and line-as-such is another existence; rather, line-as-such itself is what is predicated of it. So corporeality, with everything that is supposed, is something determinate that is only corporeality without increase. As for magnitude, there is no magnitude only, but it must have differentiate until it exists as a determinate essence, either a line or a surface.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents the first of five branches that follow from the view that the body is composed of matter (hayula) and form (sura). The branch states that every body, even celestial bodies that do not actually divide, is composed of matter. The proof is that corporeality—substantial extension—is a specific nature, not a genus. It does not differ by differentiate but only by accidents and individuators. Since in bodies that accept division, corporeality is in need of matter due to its continuous essence itself (not due to accidents), and the necessary concomitant of an essence does not vary, then all bodies must need matter. The passage then provides a verification from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa: corporeality exists externally as a determinate thing, and additional natures like hot/cold, celestial/elemental are added to it externally, not as constitutive differentiate. This is contrasted with magnitude: magnitude is not determinate until it is diversified into line or surface; it needs differentiate to exist. Corporeality, however, is determinate in itself. This contrast reinforces that corporeality is a specific nature, not a genus, and therefore its need for matter is essential and universal.

هيولي (hayula)
صورة (sura)
جسمية (jismiyya)
طبيعة نوعية (tabi'a naw'iyya)
مقدار (miqdar)
انفصال انفكاكي (infisal infikaki)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial bodies do not require matter because they do not undergo division or change.
Correction: The argument shows that the need for matter is not due to actual divisibility but due to the essence of corporeality itself, which is a specific nature. Since this need is an essential concomitant, it applies to all bodies regardless of whether they divide.

Checkpoint

C) Because they are subject to generation and corruption.
D) Because they are perceptible by the senses.

Key Takeaway
Corporeality is a specific nature, not a genus, so its essential need for matter applies to every body, including celestial spheres.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that even celestial spheres—glorious, vast, and seemingly eternal—are dependent on matter for their existence. You, too, are dependent on something outside yourself for every moment of your being. Do you live as if you are self-sufficient, or do you constantly feel your need for the Creator? What would change if you truly understood that your very essence requires Him?
?
The author distinguishes between essential necessities and accidental additions. In your life, you often define yourself by accidents: your job, your status, your health. But your essence as a human being is inherently needy. Are you investing more in maintaining accidental labels than in acknowledging your essential dependence on Allah? How can you realign your priorities?
?
Corporeality's need for matter is not because of external circumstances but because of its own nature. Similarly, your need for guidance and forgiveness is woven into your very soul. When you sin or neglect worship, are you acting as if you have no such inherent need? If you truly believed that your nature demands submission, would you ever willingly turn away from your Creator?

The Objection from Divisibility and the Answer from al-Isharaat
Objection and answer regarding need for matter due to divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between a cause (illa) and an indicator (mu'arraf) in theological argumentation, and how the capacity for division reveals an essential need for matter in all bodies.

Core Idea
The capacity for division is not the cause of needing matter—it is the sign that reveals an essential, universal need.

This passage presents a second objection to the argument that matter is required for all bodies. The objector suggests that the need for matter might be due to an accidental property—divisibility—rather than the essence of corporeality. The answer, drawn from Ibn Sina's Isharaat, clarifies that the capacity for division serves not as the cause but as an epistemological sign (mu'arraf) that uncovers an inherent need. We will trace this subtle yet crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the objection starts with 'نعم يرد' and is structured as a question.
2
Identify the objection
What exactly is the objector claiming? He says the need for matter might be due to an accident like divisibility, so it would not apply to bodies that cannot divide, like celestial spheres.
The objector accepted the specificity of corporeality but challenges the necessity of matter for all bodies.
3
Understand the answer from al-Isharaat
The answer distinguishes between cause (illa) and indicator (mu'arraf). The capacity for division is not the cause of the need, but a sign that reveals an essential need. Therefore, the need is universal, not restricted to divisible bodies.
This is a key methodological point: need is essential, and the indicator simply makes us aware of it.
4
See the reinforcement
The passage concludes that every body, by its essence, extension, and magnitude, is capable of disjunctive division (even if prevented by an accident), so matter is universal. This is acknowledged even by those who limit matter to generable and corruptible bodies.
The argument relies on the essential capacity, not actual division.
5
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why this matters: It defends the claim that all bodies, including celestial ones, require matter (hayula), which is important for arguments about creation and contingency.
This supports the view that matter is a universal constituent of bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم يرد بعد تسليم ما ذكر في بيان نوعيتها أنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك من جهة بعض العوارض كقبول الانفصال الانفكاكي، فلا يجري فيما لا يقبله كالفلكيات. وقد أشير في الإشارات إلى الجواب بأن قبول الجسمية للانفصال مع امتناع بقائها معه معرف لاحتياجها في ذاتها إلى المادة، فيفتقر إليها، حيث كانت أنه ليس علة الاحتياج ليخص الاحتياج بما يقبل الانفصال، بل علة للتصديق بالاحتياج الذاتي فيعم . ولا خفاء في توجه المنع ، وقد تقرر عموم الهيولي للأجسام بأن كل جسم فهو بالنظر إلى ذاته وامتداده ومقداره قابل للانفصال الانفكاكي، وإن كان مع امتناعه لعارض، وقد اعترف بذلك أكثر القائلين باختصاص الهيولي بالأجسام الكائنة الفاسدة. انتهى.
Yes, there arises, after accepting what was mentioned in the demonstration of its specificity, [the objection] that why may it not be that that [need for matter] is due to some accidents, like the acceptance of disjunctive division, and thus it does not apply to what does not accept it, like the celestial spheres? And it has been indicated in al-Isharaat to the answer: that the acceptance of corporeality for division while its subsistence with it is impossible is an indicator (mu'arraf) of its need in its essence for matter, so it is in need of it, since it is not the cause of the need to restrict need to what accepts division, but rather a cause for the affirmation of essential need, and thus it is universal. And it is not hidden that the refutation is sustained, and the universality of matter for bodies has been established by [the fact] that every body, with respect to its essence, extension, and magnitude, is capable of disjunctive division, even if it is prevented by an accident. And most of those who say matter is specific to generable and corruptible bodies have acknowledged this. End.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a second objection and answer. The objector, having accepted that corporeality is a specific nature, still asks: Why couldn't the need for matter be due to an accident like the capacity to be divided? If so, bodies that cannot be divided (like celestial spheres) would not need matter. The answer is drawn from Ibn Sina's Isharaat: The fact that a body accepts division while being unable to continue existing after division is not the cause of its need for matter, but rather a sign (mu'arraf) that reveals its essential need. Therefore, the need is not restricted to divisible bodies. The sign is a way for us to know that the need is essential, but the need itself is inherent. The passage then states that the universality of matter for all bodies is established because every body, by its essence, extension, and magnitude, is capable of division (even if external factors prevent it). This was acknowledged by most who claim matter is only for generable and corruptible bodies. The argument thus generalizes the need for matter to all bodies, celestial included.

Key Terms
عارض (arad)
انفصال انفكاكي (infisal infikaki)
علة (illa)
معرف (mu'arraf)
حاجة (haja)
هيولي (hayula)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the capacity for division is the cause of the need for matter.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is only a sign (mu'arraf) that reveals an essential need, not the cause (illa) itself.

Checkpoint
According to the answer from al-Isharaat, why does the need for matter apply to all bodies even though some do not divide?
A) Because all bodies actually divide in reality.
B) Because the capacity for division is a cause that applies to all.
C) Because the capacity for division is a sign of an essential need that is universal, not a cause restricted to divisible bodies.
D) Because celestial bodies are also generable and corruptible.

Key Takeaway
The capacity for division is an indicator (mu'arraf) of an essential need for matter, not the cause, so the need applies to all bodies universally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, you readily sense your need for God. But in times of ease, do you assume that need fades? This lesson teaches that your need is essential and universal—not dependent on circumstances. Where in your life have you mistaken a temporary sign (like a crisis) for the real cause of your spiritual poverty? Will you commit today to acknowledging your intrinsic need for God every moment, not just when you are 'divided' by hardship?
?
The objectors limited the need for matter to divisible bodies, but the answer exposes that as a confusion of sign with cause. In your own faith, do you ever limit your need for divine guidance to specific 'religious' areas while neglecting your essential need for Him in your thoughts, ambitions, and daily routines? What would change if you saw every aspect of your life as displaying an essential need for God's constant care?
?
The passage argues that every body is capable of division in principle, revealing a universal need. Could it be that your soul, too, has a universal and essential need for divine truth that you have been hiding behind accidental excuses? What are the 'accidents' you use to justify your spiritual negligence? Write down one excuse you will drop today, and one action you will take to affirm your essential need for God.

The Universality of Matter and Imaginary Division
Objection and response defending the universality of matter via imaginary division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the capacity for imaginary division proves that matter is a necessary component of all bodies, including celestial ones.

Core Idea
Imaginary division reveals a real need for matter in every body.

The argument from division is a cornerstone of kalam's proof for prime matter. But what about bodies like the celestial spheres that never actually divide? This lesson unpacks a sophisticated objection and its answer, showing how the very possibility of imaginary division—rooted in real potentiality—entails a permanent substrate (matter) essential to all bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objections
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the sequence: an initial objection, an answer from Avicenna's Isharat, then a second objection about imaginary division, and a final response.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow rather than every detail.
2
Understand the first objection and its response
The objector claims that the need for matter might be due to accidental divisibility, not essential. Find how the answer from the Isharat refutes this by arguing that the capacity for division reveals an essential need, not an accidental one.
The key point: the cause of the need is not the accident but the bodily nature itself.
3
Grasp the concept of imaginary division
The second objection says imaginary division is only mental. The response clarifies that such division is based on a real possibility of there being ‘part other than part’ in reality. Understand why this distinction matters for proving matter externally.
Imaginary division here is not false or arbitrary; it reflects a genuine ontological potential.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check if you can identify the key premise that imaginary division implies a real substrate.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم يرد بعد تسليم ما ذكر في بيان نوعيتها أنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك من جهة بعض العوارض كقبول الانفصال الانفكاكي، فلا يجري فيما لا يقبله كالفلكيات. وقد أشير في الإشارات إلى الجواب بأن قبول الجسمية للانفصال مع امتناع بقائها معه معرف لاحتياجها في ذاتها إلى المادة، فيفتقر إليها، حيث كانت أنه ليس علة الاحتياج ليخص الاحتياج بما يقبل الانفصال، بل علة للتصديق بالاحتياج الذاتي فيعم . ولا خفاء في توجه المنع ، وقد تقرر عموم الهيولي للأجسام بأن كل جسم فهو بالنظر إلى ذاته وامتداده ومقداره قابل للانفصال الانفكاكي، وإن امتنع ذلك لأمر زائد لازم كالصورة الفلكية أو غير ذلك لازم كغاية الصغر ،والصلابة، وفي شرح الإشارات ما يشعر بأن قبول الانفصال الوهمي كاف في إثبات المادة إذ لا بقاء للاتصال الانفصال فلا بد من قابل باق . مع واعترض بأن الانفصال الوهمي إنما يرفع الاتصال في الوهم دون الخارج، فلا يلزم وجود الهيولي في الخارج على ما هو المطلوب. وأجيب : بأن معنى إمكان الانفصال الوهمي هو أن يكون الجسم بحيث يصح حكم الوهم بأن فيه شيئاً غير شيء وجزءاً دون جزء لا من الأحكام الكاذبة الوهمية ، بل الصادقة المبنية على إمكان جزء غير جزء في نفس الأمر وهو معنى الانفصال الخارجي، وحاصله أن القسمة الوهمية، وإن لم تستلزم الانفكاكية لكن قبولها يستلزم ،قبولها ، وهو يستلزم ثبوت المادة في نفس الأمر.
Yes, it is objected after conceding what was mentioned in clarifying its specific nature: why may it not be that this [need for matter] is from the aspect of some accidents, like the acceptance of separative division, so that it does not apply to what does not accept it like the celestial bodies? And it has been indicated in the Isharat [of Avicenna] to the answer that the acceptance of division by bodily nature while its persistence with it is impossible indicates its need for matter in its essence, so it is in need of it, because it is not the cause of the need to particularize need to what accepts division, but rather it is a cause for believing in the essential need, so it is universal. And it is not hidden that the objection is possible, and it has been established that hyle [al-hayula] is universal for bodies because every body, in view of its essence and extension and quantity, is capable of separative division, even if that is prevented by an additional necessary matter like the celestial form or other necessary matter like extreme smallness and hardness. And in the commentary on the Isharat there is an indication that the acceptance of imaginary division is sufficient for proving matter, since there is no persistence of connection at division, so there must be a persistent substrate. And it was objected that imaginary division only removes connection in the imagination, not externally, so the existence of hyle externally does not follow, which is what is desired. And it was answered: The meaning of the possibility of imaginary division is that the body is such that the imagination can correctly judge that in it there is something other than something, and a part without a part, not from false imaginary judgments, but from true ones based on the possibility of a part other than a part in reality, and that is the meaning of external division. Its summary: imaginary division, even if it does not entail separation, its acceptance entails acceptance [of something], and that entails the affirmation of matter in reality.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a further objection-and-answer following the previous discussion. The objector concedes that bodily nature is a specific nature but asks: why couldn't the need for matter be due to accidental properties like the capacity for division, so that only bodies that actually undergo division need matter, not celestial bodies? The answer from Avicenna's Isharat is that the capacity for division, even if never actualized, reveals an essential need for matter in all bodies. The commentator then adds that every body, by its very essence, is divisible in principle, even if prevented by external factors. A second objection arises: imaginary division is only mental, not external, so it doesn't prove matter exists externally. The response clarifies that imaginary division here is not arbitrary but based on the real possibility of internal distinction, which corresponds to external divisibility. Thus, the capacity for such true imaginary judgment entails a real substrate (matter) in the external world.

الانفصال الوهمي

الإشارات

Misconception: Imaginary division is a purely mental exercise with no bearing on external reality.
Correction: The passage argues that imaginary division in this context is based on the real possibility of internal distinction, which entails a real substrate (matter) in the external world.

Checkpoint
According to the response in this passage, why does acceptance of imaginary division prove the existence of matter?
A) Imaginary division actually separates the body externally.
B) Imaginary division is based on the real possibility of division, implying a persistent substrate.
C) The imagination can create matter out of nothing.
D) Celestial bodies also undergo physical division.

Key Takeaway
The capacity for imaginary division, rooted in a real potential for internal distinction, proves that every body—celestial or terrestrial—requires a persistent material substrate (matter).

Objection and Answer: Can Matter Have Attributes Preparing It for Specification?
The author presents a further objection and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an objection that matter could have attributes preparing it for specification fails on the philosophers' own principles.

Core Idea
The philosophers cannot use divine choice to solve specification problems.

This lesson presents a second objection against the impossibility of matter without form. The objection asks why abstract matter cannot have attributes that ready it for a specific location when it becomes a body. The answer reveals a key inconsistency: the philosophers' own principles prevent them from appealing to God's direct will to resolve this.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the objection: why can't matter have attributes other than form that prepare it for specification?
Notice that the objection assumes matter could have such attributes.
2
Understand the answer
The answer says that attributing specification to God's choice, as al-Ghazali suggested, is not allowed on the philosophers' principles. Consider why this is a problem for them.
The answer does not reject divine power; it points out an inconsistency.
3
Reflect on the logical force
Why does this answer defeat the objection? Because the objector (a philosopher) cannot consistently invoke divine choice to explain specification. The objection thus collapses.
This shows how objections can be refuted by showing they violate the opponent's own assumptions.

نعم يتوجه أن يقال لم لا يجوز أن تكون للهيولي المجردة أوصاف وأحوال غير الصورة والأوضاع تعدها للاختصاص عند التجسم ببعض الأوضاع والأحياز على التعيين، وأما الرفع بإسناد الاختصاص إلى القادر المختار على ما ذكره الإمام فلا يتأتى على أصول القائلين بالهيولي .
Yes, it may be said: why is it not permissible that abstract hylè has attributes and states other than form and positions that prepare it for specification when it becomes embodied with some positions and places determinately? As for the removal [of the problem] by attributing specification to the omnipotent chooser as stated by the Imam, it is not consistent with the principles of the proponents of hylè.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer regarding the impossibility of matter without form. The objection asks: why can't abstract matter have attributes (other than form and position) that specially prepare it for a specific location when it becomes a body? The answer is that appealing to the Creator's direct choice (as suggested by al-Ghazali) is not acceptable on the principles of those who affirm hylè (the philosophers), because they do not allow direct divine intervention in such matters. Thus, the objection fails because it violates the assumptions of the opponent.
