Objection and Answer on Infinite Regress in Forms
Objection and answer on infinite regress in forms, distinguishing musawaqah and musabaqah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an important objection to the proof for substantial forms and how the answer distinguishes between two types of causal dependence: musawaqah (co-occurrence) and musabaqah (precedence).

Core Idea
The distinction between co-occurrence and precedence saves the proof for substantial forms from infinite regress.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection from Imam al-Razi against the earlier argument for substantial forms. The objection claims the same reasoning can be applied to any body-form specification, leading to either infinite regress or a refutation of the proof. The answer clarifies the nature of causal dependence, distinguishing between two types: musawaqah (co-occurrence) and musabaqah (precedence). By showing that accidents cannot follow the precedence model, the objection is resolved.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand how the objection parallels the previous proof for substantial forms. The objection says the same alternation applies to the specification of each body with its form.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The objection presents two ways: musawaqah (co-occurrence) leads to infinite regress; musabaqah (precedence) would refute the original proof. Notice how each leads to a different outcome.
The term 'musawaqah' means simultaneous dependence, 'musabaqah' means prior dependence.
3
Study the answer
The answer states it is on the way of precedence, but that such precedence is impossible for accidents. Two reasons are given: water heated by fire returns cold, and elemental qualities lose purity when mixed. Reflect on why these examples show accidents cannot depend on prior accidents ad infinitum.
4
Consider the caveat
The answer only holds if every accident must return after ceasing and break upon mixture. Otherwise, accidents could also be based on preserved accidents ad infinitum, like forms. Note the Imams comment in another place.
This shows the argument is not absolute but relies on empirical observations.
5
Reflect on the debate
Consider how this discussion fits into the larger kalam debate on causality and the need for substantial forms. The objection and answer strengthen the original proof by addressing a potential counterargument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بأن الترديد المذكور يجري في اختصاص كل جسم بما له الصورة، وقرره الإمام بأن اختصاص الجسم بهذه الصورة مثلاً لو كان لأجل صورة أخرى، فإما أن يكون ذلك على طريق المساوقة، فيلزم استناد كل صورة إلى صورة لا إلى نهاية أو على طريق المسابقة، بأن تستند الصورة الحاصلة في الحال إلى صورة سابقة عليها، فيندفع أصل الاحتجاج، لجواز أن يستند كل عرض إلى عرض سابق عليه. فأجاب بأنه على طريق المسابقة، ويمتنع مثله في الأعراض لأنها مستندة إلى مبادىء موجودة في الأجسام تعيدها عند زوالها بالقسر لوجهين: أحدهما: أن الماء المسخن بالنار يعود بارداً، فلولا أن في جسم الماء شيئاً محفوظ الذات عند ملاقاة النار كما كان كذلك بخلاف الصورة المائية، فإنها إذا زالت إلى الهوائية لا تعود بالطبع. وثانيهما: أن كيفيات العناصر تنكسر صرافتها عند الامتزاج، ولا كاسر سوى الصورة لما سيجيء في بحث المزاج، وأنت خبير بأن هذا إنما يتم لو ثبت لكل عرض كذلك أن يجب أن يعود بعد الزوال، وينكسر عند الامتزاج وإلا فيجوز أن تكون الأعراض التي كذلك مستندة إلى أمور محفوظة هي أعراض يستند كل منها إلى عرض قبله، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية كالصور، ولذا قال الإمام رحمه الله في موضع آخر أن الذي حصل بالدليل هو استناد هذه الأعراض من الأين،
And it was objected that the aforementioned alternation applies to the specification of each body with its form. The Imam formulated it: that the specification of a body with this form, for example, if it were because of another form, then either that would be on the way of co-occurrence, leading to each form depending on a form ad infinitum, or on the way of precedence, by the form occurring now depending on a form prior to it, in which case the original proof is refuted, because it is permissible that every accident depends on a prior accident. So he replied: that it is on the way of precedence, and the like of that is impossible in accidents, because they depend on principles existing in bodies that restore them when they cease under compulsion, for two reasons: First: that water heated by fire returns cold. Were it not that in the body of water there is something preserved in its essence upon contact with fire as it was, unlike the watery form, for when it ceases to the airy form, it does not return by nature. Second: that the qualities of the elements lose their purity when mixed, and there is no breaker except the form, as will come in the discussion of mixture. And you know that this only holds if it is established for every accident that it must return after ceasing and break upon mixture; otherwise, it is permissible that the accidents that are like that depend on preserved things that are accidents, each depending on a prior accident, and so on ad infinitum like the forms. Therefore, the Imam, may Allah have mercy on him, said in another place that what has been established by the proof is the dependence of these accidents from the 'where'...
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous proof for substantial forms and the response. The objection is that the same reasoning (alternation between two possibilities to avoid infinite regress) can be applied to the specification of each body with its form. The Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) formulated it: if a body's specification with its form is due to another form, then either this is on the way of musawaqah (co-occurrence), leading to infinite regress, or on the way of musabaqah (precedence), where the current form depends on a prior form. In the latter case, the original proof fails because one could say every accident depends on a prior accident, also avoiding infinite regress. The answer is that it is on the way of musabaqah, and that the like of that is impossible for accidents because accidents depend on principles in bodies that restore them when they cease under compulsion. Two evidences are given: (1) Water heated by fire returns cold, indicating there is a preserved essence in water that is not lost, unlike the watery form which when replaced by airy form does not return naturally. (2) The qualities of elements lose their purity when mixed, and only the form can break them, as will be explained. However, the passage notes that this answer only works if it is established for every accident that it must return after ceasing and break upon mixture; otherwise, accidents could also be based on preserved things that are themselves accidents, leading to infinite regress like the forms. So the Imam elsewhere stated that what is proven is the dependence of these accidents from the 'where' (i.e., place).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection refutes the proof for substantial forms, showing that infinite regress is unavoidable.
Correction: The answer demonstrates that the objection's second alternative (precedence) does not lead to infinite regress for forms because such precedence is impossible for accidents due to their dependence on lasting principles in bodies. Thus the proof remains valid.

Checkpoint

A) Co-occurrence and precedence
B) Essential and accidental
C) Necessary and contingent
D) Material and formal

Key Takeaway
The objection's challenge is resolved by recognizing that the causal dependence of forms is one of precedence, not co-occurrence, and this precedence model cannot generate an infinite regress for accidents because they depend on perpetual principles in bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objection suggests that the same reasoning could apply to accident specification. Have you ever allowed a similar doubt to weaken your confidence in a core belief? What would it take for you to examine the objection on its own terms, as the Imam did, and find a precise answer?
?
The Imam's answer relies on empirical observations—water returning cold and mixture—to ground a theological argument. Where in your own life have you witnessed patterns of restoration or breaking that point to deeper, unchanging principles? How can you use those observations to strengthen your faith?
?
The passage ends with a caveat: the argument only holds if certain conditions are met. This honesty requires humility. Is there an aspect of your understanding of God's creation where you have been too certain or too dismissive of nuance? How can you embrace the tension between confidence and careful qualification?

Objection and Answer: Regress of Forms and Accidents
Objection and answer regarding regress of forms and accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi defends the disjunction argument against the objection of infinite regress in forms and accidents, and the author's nuanced conclusion that accidents can succeed each other with differing preparedness.

Core Idea
Imam Razi grounds accidents in internal principles, but their succession remains possible through different preparations.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection to an earlier argument about forms. The objector argues that the same logic leads to an infinite regress for accidents. Imam al-Razi responds by distinguishing forms from accidents, using concrete examples like water heating and mixture. The author then adds his own critical assessment, leaving room for a different interpretation. By working through this dense reasoning, you'll see how kalam thinkers handled causal chains and infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening statement of the objection. The objector claims that the disjunction (between correlation and precedence) applies to the specification of any body with its form, potentially leading to an infinite regress if applied to accidents.
This objection targets an earlier argument that used a similar disjunction to prove something about forms.
2
Follow the Imam's two-part analysis
The Imam considers two possibilities: correlation (مساوقة) leading to infinite regress, or precedence (مسابقة) where the present form is grounded in a past one. He chooses precedence and argues that this is impossible for accidents because they are grounded in bodily principles that restore them after removal.
The Imam's answer relies on the idea of 'restoration by force' (القسر).
3
Examine the two reasons for impossibility in accidents
The Imam gives two reasons: (1) water heated by fire returns to cold, implying a preserved principle in water that restores coldness, unlike the water-form which changes permanently; (2) elemental qualities lose purity upon mixture, and only the form can break them. These show accidents are restored by internal principles.
The author notes these reasons only hold if every accident must return after removal and be broken upon mixture.
4
Grasp the author's critical nuance and concluding synthesis
The author argues that if the above conditions are not proven for all accidents, it is possible that some accidents are grounded in preserved entities that are themselves accidents, leading to infinite regress like forms. The Imam himself elsewhere left open whether those principles are forms or accidents. The author concludes that succession of accidents is not impossible, and differences arise from varying preparedness.
This shows the author's independent judgment within the Razi tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من واعترض بأن الترديد المذكور يجري في اختصاص كل جسم بما له الصورة، وقرر الإمام بأن اختصاص الجسم بهذه الصورة مثلاً لو كان لأجل صورة أخرى، فإما أن يكون ذلك على طريق المساوقة فيلزم استناد كل صورة إلى صورة لا إلى نهاية أو على طريق المسابقة، بأن تستند الصورة الحاصلة في الحال إلى صورة سابقة عليها، فيندفع أصل الاحتجاج، لجواز أن يستند كل عرض إلى عرض سابق عليه. فأجاب بأنه على طريق المسابقة، ويمتنع مثله في الأعراض لأنها مستندة إلى مبادئ موجودة في الأجسام تعيدها عند زوالها بالقسر لوجهين: أحدهما: أن الماء المسخن بالنار يعود بارداً، فلولا أن في جسم الماء شيئاً محفوظ الذات عند ملاقاة النار كما كان كذلك بخلاف الصورة المائية، فإنها إذا زالت إلى الهوائية لا تعود بالطبع. وثانيهما: أن كيفيات العناصر تنكسر صرافتها عند الامتزاج، ولا كاسر سوى الصورة لما سيجيء في بحث المزاج، وأنت خبير بأن هذا إنما يتم لو ثبت لكل عرض كذلك أن يجب أن يعود بعد الزوال، وينكسر عند الامتزاج وإلا فيجوز أن تكون الأعراض التي كذلك مستندة إلى أمور محفوظة هي أعراض يستند كل منها إلى عرض قبله، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية كالصور، ولذا قال الإمام رحمه الله في موضع آخر أن الذي حصل بالدليل هو استناد هذه الأعراض من الأين، والكيف، وغيرهما، إلى قوى موجودة في الجسم، وأما إنها صور. لا أعراض فلا، بل الأقرب عندنا أنها من قبيل الأعراض. والحاصل أنه كما لا يمتنع تعاقب الصور على الإطلاق، لا يمتنع الأعراض التي يستند إليها ما يعود بعد الزوال، فيكون كل سابق معداً للاحق، ويرجع اختلافها إلى اختلاف الاستعدادات، وإن كان المبدأ واحداً.
And an objection was raised that the mentioned disjunction applies to the specification of each body with its form, and the Imam established that the specification of the body with this form, for example, if it were for another form, then either that is by way of correlation, leading to the grounding of each form in a form ad infinitum, or by way of precedence, such that the form occurring now is grounded in a preceding form, then the original argument is rebutted, because it is permissible for each accident to be grounded in a preceding accident. So he answered that it is by way of precedence, and the like is impossible in accidents because they are grounded in principles existing in bodies that restore them when they are removed by force, for two reasons: One: that water heated by fire returns cold, so unless there is in the body of water something preserved in essence when meeting fire as it was, unlike the water-form, for when it passes away to the air-form, it does not return naturally. And the second: that the qualities of the elements lose their purity upon mixture, and there is no breaker except the form, as will come in the discussion of temperament. And you know that this is only established if it is proven for each accident that it must return after removal and be broken upon mixture, otherwise it is possible that accidents which are like that are grounded in preserved things that are accidents, each of which is grounded in an accident before it, and so on ad infinitum like forms. Therefore, the Imam (may God have mercy on him) said elsewhere that what is obtained by proof is the grounding of these accidents of place, quality, and others, to forces existing in the body. But as to whether they are forms, not accidents, no; rather, the closer view to us is that they are of the genus of accidents. And the summary is that just as the succession of forms is not absolutely impossible, so too the succession of accidents to which what returns after removal is grounded is not impossible. Thus, each preceding accident is a preparation for the succeeding one, and their difference reduces to difference of preparedness, even if the principle is one.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to an earlier argument about forms, followed by an answer from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, and then a concluding assessment. The objector argues that the disjunction mentioned (between correlation and precedence) applies to the specification of each body with its form. The Imam analyzes that if a body's specification with a form were due to another form, there are two possibilities: either by way of correlation (المساوقة), which would lead to an infinite regress of forms each grounding another, or by way of precedence (المسابقة), where the present form is grounded in a prior form. If it is precedence, the original argument is defeated because it would allow each accident to be grounded in a preceding accident, also leading to an infinite regress. The Imam answers that the relationship is by way of precedence, but this is impossible in the case of accidents because accidents are grounded in principles (مبادئ) existing in bodies that restore (تعيد) them when removed by force (بالقسر). He gives two reasons: first, water heated by fire returns to cold, implying there is something preserved in the water that restores its coldness, unlike the water-form which, when it changes to air-form, does not return naturally. Second, the qualities of elements lose their purity upon mixture, and nothing breaks them except the form. However, the author comments that this argument is only valid if it is proven that every accident must return after removal and be broken upon mixture. Otherwise, it is possible that those accidents are grounded in preserved entities that are themselves accidents, leading to an infinite chain like forms. So the Imam himself elsewhere said that the proof establishes that accidents like place and quality are grounded in forces in the body, but whether these forces are forms or accidents is not settled; the author says the closer view is that they are accidents. Finally, the author summarizes: just as the succession of forms is not impossible, so too the succession of accidents that ground returning phenomena is not impossible. Each preceding accident is a preparation (معد) for the succeeding one, and their differences arise from differences in preparedness (استعدادات), even if the principle is one.

What are you doing to increase your preparedness for divine grace? Are you intentionally cultivating the 'preparedness' that attracts the accidents of mercy and knowledge?

Proof That Bodies Have Substantial Forms from Their Specific Effects
Proof that bodies have substantial forms as principles of effects, with refutation of alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why bodies must have substantial forms as the principles of their specific effects, and how the author refutes alternative explanations that attribute effects directly to God or separate intelligences.

Core Idea
Specific effects require specific principles, and those principles must be forms, not accidents.

In this passage, the author presents a logical proof that bodies cannot consist only of matter and general corporeal form, because they produce distinct effects. This necessitates additional principles within bodies, which must be substantial forms. He then anticipates and refutes several objections, including the idea that effects could come directly from God or from separate intellects. This lesson walks you through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Observe the specific effects of bodies
Notice that fire burns, water moistens. These are known by necessity. Ask: What causes these effects?
The author starts with self-evident observations.
2
Identify the need for additional principles
If bodies had only matter and corporeal form, they wouldn't produce diverse effects. Therefore, there must be something else in them.
This is the core inference.
3
Determine that these principles are forms
Since bodies differ by their effects, the principles must be what make them differ. They cannot be accidents because a substance cannot be constituted by an accident.
Forms are substantial, not accidental.
4
Consider the refuted alternatives
The author rejects explanations that attribute effects directly to God, separate intelligences, or different preparednesses of matter. Understand why these are insufficient.
The refutation is implied by the constancy of effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال نحن نعلم بالضرورة أن ههنا آثاراً صادرة عن الأجسام كالإحراق للنار، والترطيب للماء، فلو لم يكن فيها إلا الهيولي والصورة الجسمية لما كان كذلك، فلا بد فيها من أمور هي مبادئ تلك الآثار ولا خفاء في أن الأجسام إنما تختلف بحسب آثارها المخصوصة بتنوع نوع، فتنوعها وتحصلها إنما يكون باعتبار تلك المبادئ، فتكون صوراً لا أعراضاً لامتناع تقوم الجوهر بالعرض. وحينئذ يندفع ما يقال : لم لا يجوز أن تكون تلك الآثار مستندة إلى الفاعل المختار؟ أو يكون لبعض المفارقات خصوصية بالقياس إلى بعض الأجسام دون بعض؟ أو يكون اختلاف الآثار عن المفارق بحسب اختلاف استعدادات الأجسام وهيوليتها؟
And it may be said: We know by necessity that there are effects issuing from bodies, such as burning from fire and moistening from water. If there were nothing in them except matter (hyle) and corporeal form, it would not be so. Therefore, there must be in them things that are the principles of those effects, and it is not hidden that bodies only differ according to their specific effects by a specific type. So their diversification and actualization is only by consideration of those principles. Thus, they are forms, not accidents, because it is impossible for a substance to be constituted by an accident. Then, what is said is rebutted: Why is it not permissible that those effects are grounded in the free agent? Or that some separate (intelligences) have a particular relationship to some bodies without others? Or that the diversity of effects from the separate (intelligence) is according to the diversity of preparednesses of bodies and their matter?
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that bodies must have substantial forms (صور نوعية) as principles of their specific effects. The author states that we know by necessity that bodies have specific effects: fire burns, water moistens. If bodies consisted only of prime matter (الهيولي) and corporeal form (الصورة الجسمية), they would not produce these distinct effects. Therefore, there must be additional principles (مبادئ) in bodies that account for these effects. Since bodies differ according to their specific effects, their diversification must be due to these principles. These principles must be forms (صور) and not accidents (أعراض), because it is impossible for a substance (الجوهر) to be constituted (تقوم) by an accident. After presenting this proof, the author refutes alternative explanations. Someone might ask: Why cannot these effects be directly from the free agent (الفاعل المختار, i.e., God)? Or why cannot some separate intellect (مفارق) have a special relation to certain bodies? Or why cannot the diversity of effects be due to different preparednesses (استعدادات) of bodies and their matter? The author implies that these alternatives are refuted by the fact that the effects are constant and intrinsic, not dependent on external volition or separate intelligences, but the refutation is not fully elaborated here. The point is that the proof for forms stands against these objections.

Key Terms
آثار
هيولي
صورة جسمية
مبادئ
جوهر
عرض
فاعل مختار
مفارقات
استعداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributing effects to God's direct action negates the need for intrinsic forms in bodies.
Correction: The constancy and specificity of effects require intrinsic principles (forms) because if effects depended solely on God's will or separate intelligences, they would not be as regular and uniform as observed.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the principles of effects in bodies must be forms and not accidents?
A) Because accidents cannot be grounded in bodies
B) Because it is impossible for a substance to be constituted by an accident
C) Because forms are more numerous than accidents
D) Because accidents are not real

Key Takeaway
Bodies must have substantial forms to account for their specific, constant effects, and these forms are substantial differences not reducible to accidents or external causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see fire burn with constancy. If you believed that God creates each act of burning directly without any intrinsic cause in the fire, how would that affect your trust in the reliability of the natural world as a sign of God’s wisdom? Are you willing to accept that you live in a world of fixed causes that reflect their Creator?
?
Think of a personal habit or reaction—like anger or patience—that seems to arise from you spontaneously. Does this passage challenge you to consider that there is an intrinsic principle (like your soul) that produces these effects, rather than just external circumstances? How would you live differently if you truly saw your actions as flowing from a stable inner nature?
?
You depend on the specific effects of water and food every day. If there were no substantial forms, those things could become poison or nourishment arbitrarily. Does the idea that things have fixed essences give you a sense of security and gratitude toward the One who set these essences? Or do you take it for granted?

Rebuttal of the Two Proofs: The Differentiator Need Not Be a Form
Rebuttal arguing that the differentiator could be an accident, not necessarily a form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rebuttal against the necessity that the differentiator must be a form, and the alternative possibilities considered in the argument.

Core Idea
The differentiator between bodies may be an accident, not necessarily a substance.

This lesson examines a critical rebuttal to the proofs that each body must have a specific form. The author concedes certain premises but challenges the conclusion that the differentiator must be a substantive form. Instead, he suggests it could be an accident inhering in a part of the body, and then counters that possibility. Through this debate, we see the rigorous nature of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concessions
The rebuttal begins by conceding that bodies differ in reality, effects issue from them, and their matter and corporeal forms are common. Recognize these as the shared ground before the objection.
These concessions are not the main point but set the stage.
2
Identify the objection
The author objects that the differentiator does not have to be a substance inhering in matter. He proposes that it could be an accident subsisting in one of the parts of the body.
This is the core alternative presented.
3
Follow the counter-rebuttal
The text then provides a counter-argument: an accident inhering in the body is posterior and constituted by the body, so it cannot be a constituent that is prior. Alternatively, if it were a non-inhering substance, it would not be a form.
The argument ends mid-sentence; consider what the conclusion might be.
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
Since the passage is cut off, think about what the next step in the argument would logically be. This highlights how kalam debates often build on each other.
This is a good opportunity to practice reconstructing arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد على هذا التقريرين بعد تسليم اختلاف الأجسام بالحقيقة، وكون الآثار صادرة عنها، وكون هيوليتها متفقة للحقيقة وكذا صورها الجسمية. إنا لا نسلم لزوم كون ما به الاختلاف جوهراً حالاً في الهيولي ليكون صورة، ولم لا يجوز أن يكون عرضاً قائماً بأحد جزئيه لا بالجسم نفسه، ليدفع بأن العرض الحال في الجسم متقوم به متأخر عنه، فلا يكون مقوماً له متقدماً عليه، أو يكون جوهراً غير حال في مادته، فلا يكون صورة، ولا يكون الاحتياج
And it is objected to these two accounts after conceding the difference of bodies in reality, and that effects issue from them, and that their matter is in agreement in reality and likewise their corporeal forms. We do not concede the necessity that that by which the difference occurs is a substance inhering in matter to be a form. And why is it not permissible that it be an accident subsisting in one of its parts and not in the body itself? This is rebutted by saying that the accident inhering in the body is constituted by it and posterior to it, so it cannot be a constituent of it and prior to it. Or that it be a substance not inhering in its matter, then it would not be a form, and the need...
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents a rebuttal (يرد) to the two previous proofs (التقريرين) for specific forms. The author first concedes certain premises: that bodies differ in reality, that effects issue from them, and that their matter and corporeal forms are common. However, he does not concede that the differentiator (ما به الاختلاف) must be a substance (جوهر) inhering in matter to be a form. He raises an alternative: why cannot the differentiator be an accident (عرض) that subsists in one of the parts (جزئيه) of the body, not in the body itself? But then he immediately presents a counter-rebuttal: an accident inhering in the body is constituted (متقوم) by the body and is posterior (متأخر) to it, so it cannot be a constituent (مقوم) of the body that is prior (متقدم) to it. Alternatively, if the differentiator were a substance not inhering in matter, then it would not be a form (since a form must inhere in matter). The passage ends mid-sentence at 'ولا يكون الاحتياج' (and the need...), presumably continuing with a conclusion. The argument shows that the debate about whether specific forms exist is not settled by the simple proofs; further analysis is required.

Misconception: The differentiator must be a substance inhering in matter (a form).
Correction: The rebuttal argues that it could be an accident subsisting in a part of the body, or a non-inhering substance, so it is not necessarily a form.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the rebuttal against the two proofs?
A) That bodies do not differ in reality
B) That the differentiator could be an accident, not necessarily a form
C) That matter and corporeal form are not common
D) That effects do not issue from bodies

Key Takeaway
The rebuttal shows that the proofs for specific forms are not decisive, because the differentiator could be an accident or a non-inhering substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that even conceded premises (bodies differ, effects issue, matter is common) do not force a single conclusion. Are there beliefs you hold that you assume are inevitable but actually depend on unexamined assumptions? What would it cost you to examine them?
?
The rebuttal does not reject the proofs entirely but proposes plausible alternatives. Is your faith strong enough to entertain counterarguments without feeling threatened? Or do you fear that questioning a proof might weaken your iman? Answer honestly.

Proof that the Natural Place of a Body is Unique
Taftazani proves that a body has only one natural place using logical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that a body has only one natural place, and how objections are addressed.

Core Idea
A single nature demands a single natural place.

In this passage, Taftazani presents a rigorous argument that a body cannot have more than one natural place. He builds on a consensus that the requirement of a single nature is a single effect, and then refutes objections by considering cases under the absence of external constraints. This lesson breaks down his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the consensus and principle
Notice that Taftazani begins by stating the agreed-upon principle: 'the requirement of the one is one.' This is the foundation of the proof.
This principle is a logical axiom.
2
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Taftazani imagines what would happen if there were multiple natural places. He considers cases with and without a constrainer, showing each leads to impossibility.
Focus on the logical contradictions he identifies.
3
Examine the objections and responses
The text raises two objections: one about specifying causes, and another about the possibility of staying motionless. See how Taftazani handles them.
He distinguishes between natural and constrained scenarios.
4
Connect to broader doctrines
The passage ends by noting al-Ījī's view that this proof presupposes hyle (prime matter), linking to the atomist debate.
This shows how metaphysical commitments affect physical proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك ليس معللاً بالجسمية ولوازمها لا بد من خصوصية، فينقل الكلام إليها ويتسلسل. قلنا : قد سبق في بحث الصور النوعية ما يزيل هذا الإشكال، واتفقوا على أن الحيز الطبيعي لا يكون إلا واحداً لأن مقتضى الواحد واحد، ولأنه لو تعدد فعند عدم القاسر إما أن يحصل فيهما وهو محال بالضرورة، أو في أحدهما فلا يكون الآخر طبيعياً، وأيضاً إذا بقي خارجاً بالقسر، فعند زوال القاسر إما أن يتوجه إليهما وهو محال، أو إلى أحدهما وفيه ميل عن الآخر فيصير المطلوب بالطبع مهروباً بالطبع، أو لا يتوجه إلى شيء منهما، فلا يكون شيء منهما طبيعياً، لا يقال يجوز أن يكون الحصول في أحدهما أو الميل إليه بحسب ما يتفق من الأسباب المخصصة، مانعاً من الآخر، لأنا نقول الكلام فيما إذا فرض خالياً عن الأسباب الخارجة. نعم يرد عليه أنه يجوز أن لا يحصل في أحدهما، ولا يتوجه إليه لامتناع الترجح بلا مرجح، وكون كل مانعاً من الآخر، بل يبقى حيث وجد. وجعل صاحب المواقف إثبات الحيز الطبيعي من فروع القول بالهيولي نظراً إلى أن القائلين بالجزء يجعلون الأجسام متماثلة، لا تختلف إلا بالعوارض.
Similarly, it is not caused by corporeality and its necessities; there must be a specificity, so the discussion transfers to it and regresses. We say: What removes this problem has already preceded in the discussion of specific forms. They agreed that the natural place can only be one because the requirement of the one is one, and because if it were multiple, then in the absence of a constrainer, either it would occur in both—which is impossible by necessity—or in one of them, so the other would not be natural. Also, if it remains outside by force, then upon the removal of the constrainer, either it would tend to both—which is impossible—or to one of them while having an inclination away from the other, so that which is naturally sought becomes naturally fled, or it would not tend to either of them, so none of them would be natural. It cannot be said that it is possible for its occurrence in one of them or its inclination to it to be according to what happens from specifying causes, preventing the other, because we say the discussion is when it is assumed free from external causes. Rather, it is objected that it is possible that it does not occur in either of them, nor does it tend to one of them, because of the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator, and each prevents the other, rather it remains where it existed. The author of al-Mawaqif made the proof of the natural place a branch of the doctrine of hyle (al-hayula), considering that those who hold the part (al-juz') make bodies similar, differing only by accidents.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that a body has only one natural place. The author (Taftazani) begins with a rejoinder to an earlier objection: the issue of specificity in corporeality is not problematic, as it was resolved in the discussion of specific forms. He then states the consensus that the natural place is singular, because a single nature demands a single effect. If there were multiple natural places, absurdities follow: without a constrainer, the body would either occupy all simultaneously (impossible), or one, leaving the other non-natural. If it remains in one by force, upon removal of the force it would either tend to all (impossible), or to one with a repulsion from the other (making the naturally desired naturally avoided), or to none (so neither is natural). An objection is considered: perhaps specification occurs due to accidental causes. The author responds that the discussion assumes absence of external causes. A further objection is noted: it is possible the body remains in its current place without motion, due to lack of a preponderator. The passage concludes by noting al-Ījī's view that proving a unique natural place depends on accepting hyle (prime matter), because atomists deny it and view bodies as fundamentally similar, differing only by accidents.

Key Terms
الحيز الطبيعي
القاسر
الترجح بلا مرجح
الهيولي
الجزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a body could have multiple natural places due to accidental specifying causes.
Correction: The passage argues that when external causes are absent, the body's single nature demands a single natural place; accidental causes cannot create a second natural place.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason the natural place of a body must be one?
A) Because bodies are composed of atoms
B) Because the requirement of a single nature is single
C) Because external causes always determine a single place
D) Because multiple places would cause the body to split

Key Takeaway
A body's unique natural place is proven by the impossibility of multiple natural places without logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that a single nature demands a single natural place. In your own life, do you have divided loyalties that pull you in different directions? How does the unity of purpose in creation call you to single-minded devotion to your Creator, and what would it take for you to remove the 'external causes' that distract you?
?
The author argues that if a body were to flee its natural place, that would be a contradiction. Are you fleeing from what you know is your true purpose (e.g., seeking Allah, fulfilling obligations) because of worldly attractions? What would it mean for you to stop fleeing and return to your natural state?
?
The objection about preponderance without a preponderator suggests that without a cause, a body remains wherever it is. In your spiritual journey, are you stagnant, waiting for a 'preponderator' to move you? What is preventing you from actively seeking your natural direction towards Allah right now?

The Dispute over Bodies Free from Accidents
Taftazani presents the two positions and their arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing positions on whether a body can be free from all accidents, their main arguments, and the author's critical response.

Core Idea
A body cannot be completely empty of accidents — or can it? Theologians disagree.

Taftazani introduces a key debate in Islamic theology: can a physical body exist without any accidental properties? He presents the views of the preventers, who say no, and the permitters, who say yes. Their arguments hinge on the nature of individuation and perception. Let's examine the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two camps
Read the opening lines to see who the preventers and permitters are, and what they claim about the possibility of a body being free from accidents.
The permitters include philosophers and Mu'tazilites.
2
Examine the preventers' argument
The preventers argue that bodies always have motion/rest and conjunction/separation, and that bodies cannot be individuated without accidents.
The author provides a response to this argument.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author says the preventers' argument does not prove the universal claim because it requires assuming that some accidents imply all others, which is false.
4
Examine the permitters' argument
The permitters give examples of bodies that seem free from certain accidents (first bodies free from conjunction/separation, air from color) and argue that non-perception without obstacle indicates non-existence.
They warn against claiming an obstacle without proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها أنه يمتنع) جميع على الجسم من العرض وضده، وجوزه بعض الفلاسفة في الأزل وبمقر المعتزلة، فيما لا يزال مطلقاً، وبعضهم في الأكوان، وبعضهم في غير الأكوان. احتج المانعون بأنها لا تخلو عن الحركة والسكون وعن الاجتماع والافتراق، وبأنها متماثلة لا تتميز ولا تتشخص إلا بالأعراض، ووجود غير المتشخص محال. والجواب: أن هذا لا يفيد العموم المتنازع إلا إذا اعتبر البعض بالبعض وهو باطل. واحتج المجوز بأن أول الأجسام خال عن الاجتماع والافتراق والهواء عن اللون، فإن عدم إدراك المحسوس بلا مانع دليل العدم، وادعاء المانع بلا بيان مفض إلى السفطة. اعلم أن ظاهر مذهبي المنع والتجويز ليسا على طرفي النقيض لأن حاصل الأول وهو مذهب أكثر المتكلمين أنه يجب أن يوجد في كل جسم أحد الضدين من كل عرض أي من كل جنس من أجناس الأعراض، إذا كان قابلاً له كذا في نهاية العقول، وقال إمام الحرمين مذهب أهل الحق أن الجوهر لا يخلو عن كل جنس من الأعراض، وعن جميع أضدادها إن كان له أضداد وعن أحد الضدين إن كان له ضد وعن واحد من جنسه إن قدر عرض لا ضد له، ولا خلاف في امتناع الخلو عن الأعراض بعد قبولها، وحاصل الثاني أنه يجوز أن لا يوجد فيه شيء من الأعراض، إما في الأزل كما هو رأى الدهرية القائلين بأن الأجسام قديمة بذواتها، محدثة بصفاتها، وإما فيما لا يزال كما هو رأى الصالحية من المعتزلة.
He said: And among them is that it is impossible for a body to be free from an accident and its opposite; some philosophers permitted it in pre-eternity, and the Mu'tazilites permitted it in the future absolutely, some of them in the accidents of location (al-akwān), and some in other than them. The preventers argued that bodies are never free from motion and rest, and from conjunction and separation, and that they are similar, not distinguished nor individuated except by accidents, and the existence of the non-individuated is impossible. The answer: This does not yield the disputed generality unless some are considered by others, which is false. The permitters argued that the first bodies were free from conjunction and separation, and air from color; for the non-perception of a sensible without an obstacle is evidence of its non-existence, and claiming an obstacle without proof leads to sophistry. Know that the apparent positions of prevention and permission are not diametrically opposed. The summary of the first, which is the doctrine of most theologians, is that in every body there must exist one of the two opposites of every accident, i.e., of every genus of the genera of accidents, if it is receptive to it, as stated in Nihayat al-'Uqul. Imam al-Haramayn said that the doctrine of the people of truth is that a substance is not free from every genus of accidents, and from all their opposites if it has opposites, and from one of the two opposites if it has a single opposite, and from one of its genus if there is no opposite. There is no dispute about the impossibility of being free from accidents after their reception. The summary of the second is that it is possible for no accident to exist in it, either in pre-eternity, as is the view of the Dahriyya who say that bodies are eternal in themselves and originated in their attributes, or in the future, as is the view of the Salihiyya of the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether a body can be completely devoid of accidents and their opposites. The author first presents the two positions: prevention (imposition of having at least one accident from each genus) and permission (possibility of being free from all accidents). He notes that the permitters have different sub-views regarding eternity, future, and specific types of accidents. Then he gives the preventers' argument: bodies always have motion/rest and conjunction/separation, and since bodies are similar, they can only be individuated by accidents; a non-individuated existence is impossible. The author answers that this argument does not prove the universal claim unless one assumes that some accidents imply all others, which is false. Next, the permitters' argument: initial bodies (like air) lacked certain accidents; non-perception without obstacle indicates non-existence; claiming an obstacle without evidence is sophistry. Finally, the author clarifies that the two doctrines are not diametrically opposed. The prevention doctrine (majority of theologians) holds that a body must have at least one of the two opposites from every genus of accidents that it is receptive to, and this is the view of Imam al-Haramayn as well. There is no dispute that after receiving accidents, a body cannot become free of them. The permission doctrine (Dahriyya and Salihiyya Mu'tazila) holds that a body can be without any accidents in pre-eternity or in the future.

Key Terms
العرض وضده
المانعون
المجوزون
السفطة
الأكوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The preventers' argument shows that every body must be full of all kinds of accidents.
Correction: The author points out that the argument only shows that some accidents are always present (like motion/rest), but not that every genus of accident is present.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the preventers' main argument for why a body cannot be free from accidents?
A) Bodies are composed of atoms that must have accidents.
B) Bodies cannot be individuated without accidents and always have motion/rest.
C) Bodies are eternal and accident-free in pre-eternity.
D) The absence of perception proves the absence of accidents.

Key Takeaway
The debate centers on whether accidents are necessary for the existence and individuation of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you were absolutely certain that something was true because it seemed obvious from the evidence you had. How might you have been like the preventers, assuming universality from a limited set of observations? What would it cost you to admit you might be wrong?
?
The permitters argued that non-perception without an obstacle proves non-existence. In your own life, where have you dismissed something as unreal simply because you couldn't see or sense it? How might this lesson urge you to pause and consider hidden obstacles before making such a judgment?
?
Taftazani warns against claiming an obstacle without proof, calling it sophistry. Consider your own arguments and beliefs: are there any 'obstacles' you have invented to avoid accepting a truth you find uncomfortable? Are you ready to let go of that sophistry and face the evidence as it is?

Clarifying the Prevention Doctrine and Its Range
Taftazani clarifies the prevention doctrine and details its requirements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced position of the prevention doctrine, which requires only one accident from each genus, not all accidents, and the sub-views of the permission doctrine.

Core Idea
The prevention doctrine is not absolute — it requires only one opposite from each genus.

After presenting the arguments, Taftazani clarifies that the two positions are not as opposed as they seem. He explains what the preventers actually claim: that a body must have at least one accident from every genus it is receptive to. This refinement resolves many apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the clarification
The author states that the two positions are not diametrically opposed. Read this carefully.
2
Study the prevention doctrine in detail
The prevention doctrine requires that in every body there must be at least one of the two opposites from each genus of accident that the body is receptive to.
This is the view of most theologians.
3
Examine Imam al-Haramayn's articulation
He adds that a substance is not free from every genus of accidents, and from all opposites if there are multiple, or one if there is a single opposite, or one from the genus if no opposite.
There is no dispute after reception.
4
Identify the sub-views of the permission doctrine
The permission doctrine holds that a body can be free from all accidents, either in pre-eternity (Dahriyya) or in the future (Salihiyya Mu'tazila).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها أنه يمتنع) جميع على الجسم من العرض وضده، وجوزه بعض الفلاسفة في الأزل وبمقر المعتزلة، فيما لا يزال مطلقاً، وبعضهم في الأكوان، وبعضهم في غير الأكوان. احتج المانعون بأنها لا تخلو عن الحركة والسكون وعن الاجتماع والافتراق، وبأنها متماثلة لا تتميز ولا تتشخص إلا بالأعراض، ووجود غير المتشخص محال. والجواب: أن هذا لا يفيد العموم المتنازع إلا إذا اعتبر البعض بالبعض وهو باطل. واحتج المجوز بأن أول الأجسام خال عن الاجتماع والافتراق والهواء عن اللون، فإن عدم إدراك المحسوس بلا مانع دليل العدم، وادعاء المانع بلا بيان مفض إلى السفطة. اعلم أن ظاهر مذهبي المنع والتجويز ليسا على طرفي النقيض لأن حاصل الأول وهو مذهب أكثر المتكلمين أنه يجب أن يوجد في كل جسم أحد الضدين من كل عرض أي من كل جنس من أجناس الأعراض، إذا كان قابلاً له كذا في نهاية العقول، وقال إمام الحرمين مذهب أهل الحق أن الجوهر لا يخلو عن كل جنس من الأعراض، وعن جميع أضدادها إن كان له أضداد وعن أحد الضدين إن كان له ضد وعن واحد من جنسه إن قدر عرض لا ضد له، ولا خلاف في امتناع الخلو عن الأعراض بعد قبولها، وحاصل الثاني أنه يجوز أن لا يوجد فيه شيء من الأعراض، إما في الأزل كما هو رأى الدهرية القائلين بأن الأجسام قديمة بذواتها، محدثة بصفاتها، وإما فيما لا يزال كما هو رأى الصالحية من المعتزلة.
He said: And among them is that it is impossible for a body to be free from an accident and its opposite; some philosophers permitted it in pre-eternity, and the Mu'tazilites permitted it in the future absolutely, some of them in the accidents of location (al-akwān), and some in other than them. The preventers argued that bodies are never free from motion and rest, and from conjunction and separation, and that they are similar, not distinguished nor individuated except by accidents, and the existence of the non-individuated is impossible. The answer: This does not yield the disputed generality unless some are considered by others, which is false. The permitters argued that the first bodies were free from conjunction and separation, and air from color; for the non-perception of a sensible without an obstacle is evidence of its non-existence, and claiming an obstacle without proof leads to sophistry. Know that the apparent positions of prevention and permission are not diametrically opposed. The summary of the first, which is the doctrine of most theologians, is that in every body there must exist one of the two opposites of every accident, i.e., of every genus of the genera of accidents, if it is receptive to it, as stated in Nihayat al-'Uqul. Imam al-Haramayn said that the doctrine of the people of truth is that a substance is not free from every genus of accidents, and from all their opposites if it has opposites, and from one of the two opposites if it has a single opposite, and from one of its genus if there is no opposite. There is no dispute about the impossibility of being free from accidents after their reception. The summary of the second is that it is possible for no accident to exist in it, either in pre-eternity, as is the view of the Dahriyya who say that bodies are eternal in themselves and originated in their attributes, or in the future, as is the view of the Salihiyya of the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether a body can be completely devoid of accidents and their opposites. The author first presents the two positions: prevention (imposition of having at least one accident from each genus) and permission (possibility of being free from all accidents). He notes that the permitters have different sub-views regarding eternity, future, and specific types of accidents. Then he gives the preventers' argument: bodies always have motion/rest and conjunction/separation, and since bodies are similar, they can only be individuated by accidents; a non-individuated existence is impossible. The author answers that this argument does not prove the universal claim unless one assumes that some accidents imply all others, which is false. Next, the permitters' argument: initial bodies (like air) lacked certain accidents; non-perception without obstacle indicates non-existence; claiming an obstacle without evidence is sophistry. Finally, the author clarifies that the two doctrines are not diametrically opposed. The prevention doctrine (majority of theologians) holds that a body must have at least one of the two opposites from every genus of accidents that it is receptive to, and this is the view of Imam al-Haramayn as well. There is no dispute that after receiving accidents, a body cannot become free of them. The permission doctrine (Dahriyya and Salihiyya Mu'tazila) holds that a body can be without any accidents in pre-eternity or in the future.

Key Terms
الأزل
الدهرية
الصالحية
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The prevention doctrine requires that a body must have both opposite accidents (e.g., heat and cold) simultaneously.
Correction: In fact, the prevention doctrine requires that for each genus of accident, at least one of the two opposites must exist, not both.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn, if an accident has a single opposite, what must a substance have?
A) Both the accident and its opposite.
B) One of the two opposites.
C) The opposite itself.
D) An accident from a different genus.

Key Takeaway
The prevention doctrine is a moderate position requiring only one accident from each genus, while the permission doctrine allows complete bareness in pre-eternity or the future.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Taftazani corrects the assumption that the two doctrines are complete opposites. In your own understanding of Islam, are there areas where you have set up false dichotomies? What would it mean for you to embrace scholarly nuance instead of oversimplifying?
?
The permission doctrine allows for a body to be without accidents, implying independence from Allah's constant creation. Do you ever act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that every moment you depend on Allah's sustaining power? How can this theological truth change your daily reliance on Him?
?
Imam al-Haramayn's precise formulation shows that even in theology, details matter. Are there aspects of your faith where you settle for vague beliefs instead of seeking clarity? What step will you take this week to deepen your understanding of a specific belief?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
4 min
Refuting the Individuation Objection Against Accidents
Sanusi presents the first objection that bodies require accidents for individuation and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that bodies need accidents for individuation is refuted by showing it only proves partial universality.

Core Idea
One accident suffices for individuation; generalization from part to whole is invalid.

Theologians argued that accidents are necessary for bodies because without them bodies would be indistinguishable, but this argument only works if you assume that every accident is needed for every body. Sanusi shows that the objection fails because it overgeneralizes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the clear structure of objection and response.
2
Identify the logical flaw
The objection argues that without accidents, bodies cannot be individuated. The refutation points out that this only proves that some accidents are needed, not all.
The key term is 'partial universality' vs 'total universality'.
3
Understand the concept of partial vs total universality
Partial universality means a property applies to some members; total universality means it applies to all. The objection erroneously concludes total universality from a partial premise.
4
Reflect on the refutation's significance
This refutation preserves the necessity of accidents for individuation while rejecting the overreaching claim that bodies must have every kind of accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والافتراق، وبأنها متماثلة لا تتميز ولا تتشخص إلا بالأعراض، ووجود غير المتشخص محال . والجواب : أن هذا لا يفيد العموم (۱) المتنازع إلا إذا اعتبر البعض بالبعض وهو باطل واحتج المجوز بأن أول الأجسام خال عن الاجتماع والافتراق والهواء عن اللون، فإن عدم إدراك المحسوس بلا مانع دليل العدم، وادعاء المانع بلا بيان مفض إلى السفطة (٢) اعلم أن ظاهر مذهبي المنع ، والتجويز ليسا على طرفي النقيض لأن حاصل الأول وهو مذهب أكثر المتكلمين أنه يجب أن يوجد في كل جسم أحد الضدين من كل عرض أي من كل جنس من أجناس الأعراض ، إذا كان قابلاً له كذا في نهاية العقول، وقال إمام الحرمين (۳) مذهب أهل الحق أن الجوهر لا يخلو عن كل جنس . من الأعراض، وعن جميع أضدادها إن كان له أضداد وعن أحد الضدين إن كان له ضد وعن واحد من جنسه إن قدر عرض لا ضد له، ولا خلاف في امتناع الجلو" عن الأعراض بعد قبولها، وحاصل الثاني أنه يجوز أن لا يوجد فيه شيء من الأعراض، إما في الأزل كما هو رأى الدهرية القائلين بأن الأجسام قديمة بذواتها ، محدثة بصفاتها، وإما فيما لا يزال كما هو رأى الصالحية من المعتزلة . (۱) في (ب) العدم بدلاً من (العموم) (۲) أصل هذا اللفظ في اليونانية (سو فيسما) وهو مشتق من لفظ (سوفوس) ومعناه الحكيم والحاذق . والسفسطة عند الفلاسفة هي الحكمة المموهة، وعند المنطقيين هي القياس المركب من الوهميات، والغرض منه تغليط الخصم واسكاته. وقيل: إن السفسطة قياس ظاهره الحق وباطنه الباطل، ويقصد به خداع الآخرين أو خداع النفس، فإذا كان القياس كاذباً ولم يكن مصحوباً بهذا القصد لم يكن سفسطة بل كان مجرد غلط أو انحراف عن المنطق . وتطلق السفسطة أيضاً على القياس الذي تكون مقدماته صحيحة ونتائجه كاذبة لا ينخدع بها أحد الا انك إذا أنعمت النظر فيه وجدته مطابقاً لقواعد المنطق. ووجدت نفسك عاجزاً عن دحضه كسفسطة السهم وسفسطة كومة القمح فإن الغرض منهما إثارة المشكلات المنطقية . راجع) کشاف اصطلاحات الفنون للتهانوي (۳) هو عبد الملك بن عبد الله بن يوسف بن محمد الجويني، أبو المعالي ركن الدين الملقب بإمام الحرمين راجع) وفيات الاعيان ۱ : ۲۸۷ والسبكي ٣ : ٢٤٩ ومفتاح السعادة ١ : ٤٤٠) (٤) في (ب) العدد بدلا من (الخلو)
And [they argued] that [bodies] are similar, not distinguished nor individuated except by accidents, and the existence of that which is not individuated is impossible. And the answer: that this does not prove the contested universality unless part is considered by part, which is invalid. And the one who permits [freedom from accidents] argued that the first of bodies is free from combination and separation, and air from color, for the non-perception of the perceptible without a hindrance is evidence of non-existence, and claiming a hindrance without clarification leads to sophistry. Know that the apparent [positions] of prohibition and permission are not opposite extremes, because the summary of the first – which is the doctrine of most theologians – is that it is necessary that in every body there exists one of two opposites from every accident, i.e., from every genus of the genera of accidents, if it is receptive to it, as in Nihayat al-‘Uqul. And Imam al-Haramayn said: The doctrine of the people of truth is that the substance is not free from every genus of accidents, and from all their opposites if it has opposites, and from one of the two opposites if it has one opposite, and from one of its genus if an accident with no opposite is supposed. And there is no disagreement about the impossibility of being free from accidents after accepting them. And the summary of the second is that it is permissible that nothing of accidents exist in it, either in pre-eternity as is the view of the Dahriyya who say that bodies are eternal in their essences, originated in their attributes, or in perpetuity as is the view of the Salihiyya of the Mu‘tazila. (1) In (b) 'non-existence' instead of 'universality'. (2) The origin of this word in Greek is (sophisma) derived from (sophos) meaning wise and skilled. And sophistry according to philosophers is specious wisdom, and according to logicians is a syllogism composed of imaginative premises, the purpose of which is to deceive the opponent and silence him. It is said that sophistry is a syllogism whose appearance is truth and its interior is falsehood, and it is intended to deceive others or oneself. If the syllogism is false and not accompanied by this intent, it is not sophistry but mere error or deviation from logic. Sophistry is also applied to a syllogism whose premises are true and conclusions false, which no one would be deceived by, but if you examine it closely you find it conforms to the rules of logic, and you find yourself unable to refute it, like the sophism of the arrow and the sophism of the heap of wheat; their purpose is to raise logical problems. (Cf. Kashshaf Istilahat al-Funun by al-Tahanawi) (3) He is ‘Abd al-Malik ibn ‘Abd Allah ibn Yusuf ibn Muhammad al-Juwayni, Abu al-Ma‘ali Rukn al-Din, nicknamed Imam al-Haramayn. (Cf. Wafayat al-A‘yan 1:287 and al-Subki 3:249 and Miftah al-Sa‘ada 1:440) (4) In (b) 'the number' instead of 'the being free'.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the necessity of accidents in bodies and their refutations, followed by a clarification of the two main positions (man‘ and tajwiz) and a statement of the correct view. The first objection argues that bodies are indistinguishable without accidents, so their existence without accidents would entail the existence of something non-individuated, which is impossible. The answer is that this argument only proves that some accident is needed for individuation, not that every body must have one of every pair of opposites—unless one considers part by part (i.e., generalizes from a single case), which is invalid. The second objection claims that the first body (the primordial body) was free from combination and separation, and air was free from color, citing that the failure to perceive a sensible without a hindrance is evidence of its non-existence. The reply is that claiming a hindrance without proof leads to sophistry, which is a deceptive argument. The passage then transitions to explaining the two positions: the doctrine of prohibition (man‘) held by most theologians requires that every body must have one of two opposites from every genus of accidents it can receive, while the doctrine of permission (tajwiz) allows a body to be free from all accidents either in the beginning (as the Dahriyya hold) or perpetually (as the Salihiyya Mu‘tazila hold). The correct view, according to Imam al-Haramayn, is that a substance is never free from every genus of accidents—if an accident has an opposite, the substance must have one of them; if no opposite, it must have one of its genus. This lesson refutes two key objections by showing they rely on invalid generalizations or lead to sophistry.

Key Terms
اجتماع
افتراق
تشخص
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that accidents are necessary in all bodies for all aspects of existence.
Correction: The objection only proves that some accidents are needed for individuation, not that every body must have every kind of accident.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw in the objection according to the refutation?
A) It assumes bodies are similar.
B) It overgeneralizes from part to whole.
C) It is based on sense perception.
D) It relies on sophistry.

Key Takeaway
The individuation argument fails because it generalizes from a partial case to a universal rule.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a conviction you hold about your faith or life that you've never seriously questioned. Could it be based on a generalization from a few cases—like the objectors did—rather than on the whole truth? What would it cost you to honestly examine that today?
?
You have seen that even a seemingly strong philosophical objection can fail when its reach exceeds its grasp. When you are presented with an argument against your beliefs, do you have the humility to admit when it has only partial force, or do you automatically reject it? How will you practice discernment to separate valid parts from invalid generalizations?
?
Sanusi refuted an overreaching claim by pointing out that part cannot stand for whole. Is there an area in your life where you have allowed a partial experience—a failure, a sin, a blessing—to define your entire view of Allah's mercy or justice? Will you commit to letting the whole of revelation, not just a part, shape your understanding?

Causality and Created Things

Second Objection and the Two Positions on Accidents
Sanusi presents the second objection about first bodies being free from accidents and then expounds the two positions and the correct view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that first bodies are free from accidents, and the two main positions (man' and tajwiz) along with the correct view of Imam al-Haramayn.

Core Idea
The second objection leads to sophistry; the correct view is that a substance must have an accident from every genus.

The second objection argues that primordial bodies were free from accidents, citing examples like air lacking color. Sanusi warns that claiming a hindrance without proof leads to sophistry. He then clarifies the two positions: the prohibitionists require accidents from every genus, while the permissivists allow complete freedom from accidents. The correct position is that a substance must have one opposite from each genus.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and its refutation
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the example of air lacking color and the response about sophistry.
2
Understand the warning against sophistry
The objector claims non-perception without a hindrance proves non-existence. The refutation says that claiming a hindrance without proof is sophistry, a deceitful argument.
Sophistry is a false argument that appears true.
3
Learn the two positions: man' and tajwiz
The first position (man') holds that every body must have one opposite from every genus of accidents. The second (tajwiz) allows bodies to be free from all accidents at some time.
4
Study the correct view by Imam al-Haramayn
Imam al-Haramayn states that a substance is never free from every genus of accidents: it must have one opposite if there is an opposite, or one of its genus if no opposite exists.
This is the balanced position between the extremes.

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the necessity of accidents in bodies and their refutations, followed by a clarification of the two main positions (man‘ and tajwiz) and a statement of the correct view. The first objection argues that bodies are indistinguishable without accidents, so their existence without accidents would entail the existence of something non-individuated, which is impossible. The answer is that this argument only proves that some accident is needed for individuation, not that every body must have one of every pair of opposites—unless one considers part by part (i.e., generalizes from a single case), which is invalid. The second objection claims that the first body (the primordial body) was free from combination and separation, and air was free from color, citing that the failure to perceive a sensible without a hindrance is evidence of its non-existence. The reply is that claiming a hindrance without proof leads to sophistry, which is a deceptive argument. The passage then transitions to explaining the two positions: the doctrine of prohibition (man‘) held by most theologians requires that every body must have one of two opposites from every genus of accidents it can receive, while the doctrine of permission (tajwiz) allows a body to be free from all accidents either in the beginning (as the Dahriyya hold) or perpetually (as the Salihiyya Mu‘tazila hold). The correct view, according to Imam al-Haramayn, is that a substance is never free from every genus of accidents—if an accident has an opposite, the substance must have one of them; if no opposite, it must have one of its genus. This lesson refutes two key objections by showing they rely on invalid generalizations or lead to sophistry.

Key Terms
سفسطة
دهرية
صالحية
منع
تجويز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second objection validly proves that bodies can be free from accidents entirely.
Correction: The objection relies on an unsupported claim of a hindrance, which is sophistry. The correct view is that a substance must have an accident from every genus.

Checkpoint
What is the position of the Dahriyya regarding accidents?
A) They believe bodies originated with accidents.
B) They believe bodies are eternal in essence but have accidents temporally.
C) They believe bodies can be free from accidents forever.
D) They deny accidents entirely.

Key Takeaway
The second objection fails because it lacks proof and leads to sophistry; the correct view requires a body to have an accident from every genus it can receive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed something does not exist simply because you have not perceived it? How does Sanusi's refutation of the objection challenge your own reasoning patterns? Identify one area where you need to seek evidence instead of relying on absence of perception, and commit to doing so this week.
?
The passage warns against claiming a hindrance without proof. Are there beliefs you hold about God, the unseen, or your own life that you justify by an 'unseen hindrance' without evidence? Write down one such belief and examine how you can seek true knowledge about it.
?
The correct doctrine that every substance must have an accident from every genus emphasizes God's constant act of creation. How does this truth affect your awareness of Allah's presence in your daily life? What specific habit can you adopt to remind yourself that every moment you are sustained by His power?

First Argument of the Deniers and Its Limited Scope
First argument from time and multiplicity for necessary accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument used by those who deny that bodies can be free from accidents, and why it only proves partial affirmation and refutes only specific positions.

Core Idea
The deniers' first argument only proves some accident must exist, not every kind.

In this lesson, we examine the first argument presented by the deniers (al-māni‘ūn) who claim that a body cannot be free from accidents. The argument relies on the body's existence in time and its multiplicity in number. However, as the author shows, this argument only establishes that a body must have some accident, not that it must have each and every type of accident. It is useful for refuting those who deny all accidents (universal negation) and the Baghdadis who allow freedom from non-color accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Note the key terms like 'حركة', 'سكون', 'اجتماع', 'افتراق'.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The argument says: body is in time and multiple, so it cannot be free from motion/stasis and combination/separation. The author then evaluates: it only gives partial affirmation, not universal.
Partial affirmation means it proves that some accident must exist, but not necessarily every kind.
3
Understand the refutation targets
The argument is sufficient to refute those who hold universal negation (deny all accidents) and the Baghdadis (who allow freedom from non-color accidents).
4
Discuss the footnote on 'salb'
The footnote defines 'salb' (negation) as removal of existential relation. It distinguishes between negation of universality and universality of negation.
This technical definition is crucial for the argument's scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجاج المانعين بأن الجسم متحقق في الزمان ومتكثر بالعدد فلا يخلو عن حركة أو سكون، واجتماع أو اقتراق على تقدير .تمامه إنما يفيد هذا الإيجاب الجزئي لا الإيجاب الكلي المدعي. نعم يصلح للرد على القائلين بالسلب (۱) الكلي، وعلى البغدادية القائلين بجواز الخلو عما عدا الألوان، وكذا احتجاجهم بأن الشيء لا يوجد بدون التشخص ضرورة، وتشخص الأجسام إنما هو بالأعراض لكونها متماثلة لتألفها من الجواهر المتماثلة، فلو وجدت بدون الأعراض لزم وجود الغير المتشخص وهـو محال لا يفيد العموم. أعني امتناع الجسم بدون أحد الضدين من كل عرض، لأن البعض كاف في التشخص . نعم يفيد عموم الأوقات. أعني الأزل(۲)، وما لا يزال بخلاف الأول فإنه ربما يمنع امتناع خلو الجسم في الأزل عن الحركة والسكون بل إنما يكون ذلك في الزمان الثاني والثالث وعن الاجتماع والافتراق، بل إنما يكون ذلك على تقدير تحقق جسم آخر، فيحتاج في التعميم إلى قياس ما قبل الاتصاف أعني الأزل على ما بعده، أعني ما لا يزال كما يقاس بعض الأعراض على البعض تعميماً للدليلين في جميع الأعراض، وتقريره أن اتصاف الجوهر بالعرض إما لذاته وإما لقابليته له ونسبة كل منهما إلى جميع الأعراض والأزمان على السوية والجواب منع المقدمتين . (۱) السلب مقابل الايجاب والمراد به مطلقاً رفع النسبة الوجودية بين شيئين (ابن سينا، النجاة : ص (۱۸) وقد يراد بالايجاب والسلب الثبوت واللاثبوت فثبوت شيء لشيء إيجاب، وانتفاؤه عنه سلب، وقد يعبر عنهما بوقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها . والسلب في القضية الحملية هو الحكم بلا وجود محمول لموضوع فالقضية الموجبة ما اشتملت على الايجاب والقضية السالبة ما اشتملت على السلب وسلب العموم نفي الشيء عن جملة الأفراد، لا عن كل فرد وعموم السلب بالعكس كليات أبي البقاء) (۲) سقط من (أ) جملة (أعني الأزل)
And the argument of those who forbid that the body is realized in time and multiple in number, therefore it is not free from motion or stasis, and combination or separation, upon its completion. This only serves this partial affirmation, not the alleged universal affirmation. Indeed, it is suitable for refuting those who hold universal negation, and the Baghdadis who hold permissibility of being free from other than colors. Likewise, their argument that a thing cannot exist without individuation necessarily, and the individuation of bodies is only by accidents because they are similar, composed of similar atoms, so if they existed without accidents, the existence of the non-individuated would be necessary, and that is impossible. [But] it does not serve universality—I mean the impossibility of a body being without one of two opposites from every accident—because a part suffices for individuation. Indeed, it serves generality of times—I mean pre-eternity and perpetuity—unlike the first [argument], for it perhaps does not prevent the possibility of a body being free in pre-eternity from motion and stasis, but rather that is only in the second and third time, and from combination and separation, but rather that is only upon the assumption of another body. So generalization requires analogizing what is before qualification (i.e., pre-eternity) to what comes after (i.e., perpetuity), just as some accidents are analogized to others to generalize both arguments to all accidents. Its formulation: the substance's qualification by an accident is either due to its essence or due to its receptivity to it, and the relation of each to all accidents and times is equal. And the answer: both premises are denied. (1) 'Negation' is the opposite of 'affirmation'; it means absolutely the removal of the existential relation between two things (Ibn Sina, al-Najat, p. 18). And 'affirmation' and 'negation' may mean existence and non-existence: the existence of a thing for a thing is affirmation, its non-existence is negation; they may be expressed as the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence. Negation in a predicative proposition is the judgment of non-existence of a predicate for a subject; the affirmative proposition is that which contains affirmation, the negative proposition is that which contains negation. Negation of universality is the denial of a thing from the totality of individuals, not from every individual, and the universality of negation is the opposite. (Abu al-Baqa' compendia.) (2) Missing from (a) the phrase 'I mean pre-eternity'.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage examines the arguments of those who forbid bodies being free from accidents (the man‘ position) and shows their limited scope. The first argument: since a body exists in time and is multiple in number, it cannot be free from motion/stasis and combination/separation. The author notes that this only proves partial affirmation (that some accident is needed) not the universal claim. However, it does refute those who deny all accidents (universal negation) and the Baghdadis who allow freedom from non-color accidents. The second argument: individuation is necessary for existence, and bodies are individuated by accidents because their constituent atoms are similar. So without accidents, they would lack individuation, which is impossible. But this argument also does not prove the universal claim (that a body must have one of each pair of opposites for every accident) because a single accident suffices for individuation. However, it does prove temporal generality: a body cannot be free from accidents at any time (pre-eternity or perpetuity). The author notes that the first argument does not necessarily prevent pre-eternal freedom from motion/stasis, but only after time begins. To extend the arguments to all accidents and all times, one would need to analogize pre-eternity to subsequent times, and analogize some accidents to others, based on the premise that the substance's relation to all accidents and times is equal. The author concludes that both premises of this analogical extension are denied. This lesson demonstrates that the arguments of the deniers only support partial affirmation and temporal continuity, not the universal claim about all accidents.

سكون
اجتماع
افتراق
إيجاب جزئي
إيجاب كلي
سلب كلي
بغدادية
سلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many mistakenly think that the first argument proves that a body must have every type of accident (e.g., must have motion or stasis, and also must have combination or separation, etc.).
Correction: The author explicitly states the argument only gives partial affirmation—it proves that some accident exists, but not that every opposite pair must be instantiated. It cannot be used to claim that a body must have one of each pair of opposites for all kinds of accidents.

Checkpoint
What does the author say the first argument of the deniers proves?
A) That a body must have one of each pair of opposites for every accident.
B) That a body must have some accident, but not every kind.
C) That a body can be free from all accidents.
D) That motion and stasis are the only possible accidents.

Key Takeaway
The first argument of the deniers only proves that a body must have some accident, not that it must have every kind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical argument, do you assume it proves more than it actually does? Reflect on a time you overextended an argument. Will you now examine its scope as the author did here?
?
The deniers' argument, though limited, still refutes those who deny all accidents. Is there a partial truth in your own life that you have dismissed because it wasn't universal? Will you revisit it with humility?
?
This lesson distinguishes between partial and universal affirmation. Can you identify an area of your faith where you have taken a partial proof and treated it as universal? What would it cost you to adjust that view?
