Second Argument from Individuation and Its Temporal Generality
Second argument from individuation and its temporal scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument of the deniers that bodies require accidents for individuation, and why it only proves that a body cannot be free from accidents at any time, not that it must have every kind.

Core Idea
Individuation via accidents ensures bodies always need some accident, but not each opposite pair.

This lesson continues with the second argument of the deniers, which appeals to the necessity of individuation. Since bodies are composed of similar atoms (jawahir mutamāthila), their individuation must come from accidents. Without accidents, they would lack individuation. However, the author demonstrates that this argument only establishes that a body must have accidents at all times (pre-eternity and perpetuity), not that it must have one of each pair of opposites for every accident. The lesson also examines the attempt to generalize this argument to all accidents through analogy and the rebuttal that both premises of that generalization are denied.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key terms 'تشخص', 'جواهر متماثلة', 'غير المتشخص'.
2
Understand the argument from individuation
The argument: a thing cannot exist without individuation; bodies are individuated by accidents because their atoms are similar; without accidents, they would be non-individuated, which is impossible.
This shows that accidents are necessary for the existence of bodies.
3
Distinguish between two types of universality
The author notes the argument does not prove per-accident universality (each opposite pair) but does prove temporal universality (at all times). A single accident suffices for individuation.
Per-accident: must have motion OR stasis for every motion-related accident? No. Temporal: at every time, some accident exists.
4
Examine the generalization via analogy
To extend the argument to all accidents and all times, one would need to analogize pre-eternity to later times and some accidents to others. The proposed premise: the substance's relation to all accidents and times is equal.
5
Learn the refutation
The author answers that both premises of the analogical extension are denied (man‘ al-muqaddimatayn).
This means the generalization fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

How does this reframe your view of your own existence from something self-sufficient to something perpetually dependent? Will you acknowledge that dependency today in a concrete action?

The First Argument: No Compulsion in Creation
First argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the inability to bring about a logical impossibility does not detract from God's power or free choice.

Core Idea
Logical impossibility is not a limitation on divine power.

The first argument attempts to show that if bodies necessarily required accidents, God would be forced to create them against His free will. Taftazani responds by distinguishing between true inability and logical impossibility, a crucial distinction in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the claim and the response.
Notice the structure: objection followed by 'الجواب'.
2
Grasp the logical structure
The argument: if body cannot be empty of accident, then God must create accident → compulsion. The response: this is like requiring the existence of a cause without its effect, which is impossible, and inability to do the impossible is not weakness.
Think of 'المحال' as what is inherently impossible.
3
Reflect on the implications
Why does inability to do the impossible not contradict choice? Because choice operates within the realm of possibility; the impossible is outside it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بجواز خلو الجسم عن الضدين في الجملة الأول: أنه لو لم يجز لكان الباري تعالى مضطراً عند خلق الجسم إلى خلق العرض، وهو ينافي الاختيار. والجواب : أن عدم القدرة على المحال كوجود الملزوم بدون اللازم لا يوجب العجز وسلب الاختيار. الثاني : أنه لو لم يجز خلو الجسم عن الاجتماع والافتراق لما جاز أن يخلق الله تعالى جسماً هو أول الأجسام بحسب الزمان واللازم قطعى البطلان . الثالث: أنه لو لم يجز خلوه عن جميع الألوان لما وقع وقد وقع كالهواء ، لا يقال : لا نسلّم خلوه عن اللون، بل غايته عدم الإحساس به، لأنـا نقـول عدم الإحساس بما من شأنه الإحساس به مع سلامة الحاسة وسائر الشرائط دليل على عدمه. فإن قيل : من جملة الشرائط انتفاء المانع وتحققه ممنوع . قلنا : فتح هذا الباب يؤدي إلى جواز أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال شامخة، وأصوات هائلة ولا ندركها لمانع . وقد يجاب بأن الشفيف ضد اللون لا عدم.
And those who argued for the permissibility of a body being empty of the two opposites argued in summary with [several] points: First: that if it were not permissible, then the Exalted Creator would be compelled when creating the body to create the accident, and this contradicts choice. The answer: that inability to do the impossible, like the existence of the necessary condition without the necessary consequence, does not necessitate inability and removal of choice. Second: that if it were not permissible for a body to be empty of composition and separation, then it would not be permissible for God the Exalted to create a body that is the first of bodies in time, and the consequent is necessarily false. Third: that if it were not permissible for it to be empty of all colors, then it would not occur, and it has occurred like air. It cannot be said: we do not concede it is empty of color; rather its utmost is the absence of sensing it, because we say: the absence of sensing something that is normally sensed, given the soundness of the sense and all conditions, is evidence of its non-existence. So if it is said: among the conditions is the absence of an obstacle, and its presence is contested. We say: opening this door leads to the permissibility that there be before us lofty mountains and tremendous sounds that we do not perceive due to an obstacle. And it may be answered that transparency is the opposite of color, not non-existence.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (وجوه) used by those who claim that a body can be empty of both opposites (e.g., color and its absence, composition and separation). The first argument claims that if a body necessarily required an accident upon creation, then God would be compelled to create that accident, which contradicts His voluntary (اختِيار) nature. The author responds (والجواب) that the inability to create the impossible (المحال) – such as the existence of the foundation (ملزوم) without its necessary property (لازم) – does not imply inability (عجز) or removal of choice; it is simply a logical impossibility, not a deficiency. The second argument is stated without an explicit answer here: if a body could not be empty of composition and separation, then God could not create the first body in time (since it would have to have these attributes before anything existed), a self-evident falsity (قطعى البطلان). The third argument uses air as an example of a body that appears colorless; the opponent objects that we do not concede air is truly colorless, but only that we do not sense its color. The author rebuts (لأنا نقول) that the absence of sensation when the sense and conditions are intact proves absence (عدم) of the color. The opponent further objects that one condition is the absence of a hindrance (مانع), which is not proven. The author counters (قلنا) that accepting this would allow absurdities like mountains and sounds present but imperceptible. Finally, an alternative answer is mentioned: transparency (الشفيف) is a color opposite, not the absence of color.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's inability to create a body without an accident would be a limitation on His power.
Correction: Inability to do the impossible does not constitute weakness; it is not within the realm of power.

Checkpoint
According to the author's response to the first argument, why does the inability to bring about a necessary conditional without its consequent not contradict God's choice?
A) Because God chooses to create the accident voluntarily.
B) Because the inability to do the impossible does not constitute weakness or remove choice.
C) Because the accident can be created later.
D) Because the body already contains the accident essentially.

Key Takeaway
Inability to do what is logically impossible does not challenge God's omnipotence or free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that seems impossible to bear, do you secretly believe that God has failed you or is not powerful enough? How does knowing that logical impossibility is not a real limitation on God change the way you view that trial — and what would it take for you to actually surrender it to Him today?
?
You claim to believe that God is free in His actions, but when His commands restrict your desires, do you feel He is taking away your freedom? Use the argument in this lesson: if obeying a necessary command (like prayer) is not compulsion but logical consistency in the created order, what would change if you lived that belief tomorrow?
?
The argument says inability to do the impossible does not negate choice. Think of an area in your life where you feel stuck — a sin pattern or a difficult situation. Are you blaming God for not giving you a way out, when in fact the impossibility is part of your created nature? What would it mean to accept that limitation and turn to Him instead of resenting Him?
